Although the songs are generally shorter, they still retain an epic scale with rapid changes. This brevity is indeed a welcome change of approach here, and this album is probably the least repetitive of their efforts. So if a song, or part of a song, doesn't appeal to you, don't worry, you'll be hearing something very different in no time!<br /><br />Despite what some others have said, I feel there still are heavy riffs to be had here, if not as bone jarring as on previous albums. The guitar is definitely thinner but crystal clear on this release, while the bass is very up front (nice to hear Martin so clearly). The guitar solos are tasteful and of course Fred shreds slickly, if sparingly. The song takes priority over lengthy solos, in contravention of most prog rock of the 1970s. Axe's drumming has really matured and he does some tasteful and inventive things on this album (take the soft double time on \\"Nepenthe\\" for instance).<br /><br />Back to that assertive keyboard in the mix -- Now that Per is gone, I wonder if this new direction will stick? And I wouldn't freak out over the lack of death vocals on this album -- Mike's vocals have improved much over the last few years and more agreeable to my ears. 1416550550|5.0|\\"Wow\\" seems such an incapable word for such an excellent, well-written, and amazing book. But wow.  Yeah, it was simply that good. It was the right mix of family and history, love and relationships, and mystery and intrigue. The characters were likeable, or dislikeable, as the case may be, and I was drawn in from the very first chapter and itching to reach the ending. Although I admittedly was sad once I did. Every once in a while you run into a novel that was so good you'd recommend it to almost anyone, and I can easily say The Forgotten Garden is one of them. I ask myself how did I not hear of Kate Morton before?  As for Morton, I could not believe how seamlessly the transitions seemed to take place from one chapter, going back and forth in time from 1900 to 2005, and many places in between. Bravo to her for that. I never lost track and the clear transition seems to becoming a lost art. Also worth mentioning was the fictional stories written by Eliza Makepeace interspersed throughout the novel. Those in themselves were well written and added immeasurably to the story itself. Sometimes in novels those added pieces take away; these in no doubt enhanced the overall story.  I just cannot believe how much I enjoyed this book. When I first picked it up, it was because the plot seemed so interesting and it seemed a fun distraction that would keep me going. It was happily both.  If I could complain about one thing, despite the fact that it had to end, it would be how wonderfully drawn out the book was - not too long in my mind at all - and how everything just seemed to come to its ending almost abruptly in the final chapters. I have no regrets on that, and maybe I would have not enjoyed the book so much if it was not paced differently, but in some ways I feel it sped up too much at the end and every revelation was coming at me all at once.  Overall, one of the best books I've read in a long time, once I will encouragingly recommend. And definitely being added to my list of favorites. I look forward to re-reading The Forgotten Garden at some point in the future and also reading the author's other books.  Again, bravo, Kate Morton. Thank you. And, yes, Wow.

I don't normally like to go into too many plot details in reviews, but because the plot so colored how I felt about the book, I am doing that here.<br /><br />\\"The Thorn Birds\\" takes three generations of the Cleary family from Wahine, New Zealand, to an enormous Australian Outback sheep station called Drogheda to London in 1968. Meggie, the main character, is four when the story starts in 1915 and the only girl in a poor rural family of six children. Her father Paddy is an itinerant farm worker who emigrated from Galway. Her mother Fiona is the scion New Zealand's wealthiest founding family. The eldest Cleary son Frank is dark, angry and different from his red-headed younger brothers and sister. Only out of love for Fiona do Frank and Paddy control their dislike of each other. Fiona, cut off from her family and its wealth but clinging to the dignity, manners and a few material remnants of her genteel upbringing, bears some secret sorrow and never displays emotion of any kind. This puritanical, hard-working, tired family never shows much affection, leaving the hyper-sensitive, volatile Frank as Maggie's only source of emotional comfort.<br /><br />As Meggie reaches age 10, the Clearys' lives change when Paddy's elder sister, Mary Carson, invites Paddy to manage Drogheda in New South Wales. Mary, a wealthy childless widow, promises Paddy he will be her heir. When they arrive at Drogheda, the Clearys are greeted by the startlingly attractive local Catholic parish priest, Ralph de Bricassart, who forms an immediate bond with Meggie. Father Ralph, who once dreamed of rising to great heights in the Church, has been sent to the Outback as punishment for having insulted a bishop. He has been working on Mary Carson for some time to try to get her to leave enough of her vast wealth to the Church so he will win the approval of his superiors and escape his rural purgatory. However, Father Ralph comes to so love Meggie and her family that he sets those plans aside.<br /><br />After the Clearys have worked at Drogheda at low pay for several years in anticipation of of their inheritance, Mary Carson throws a great birthday party for herself. Meggie has developed a crush on Father Ralph, and he knows it. At the party, Father Ralph sees that Meggie is no longer a child but a beautiful young woman. That night Mary, who is furiously jealous of Father Ralph's love for Meggie, gives Father Ralph a new will and tells him to open it after he sees her dead body but before he buries her. Father Ralph meets Meggie outside the party and they share a passionate kiss, but he tells Meggie he can never leave the Church. Mary dies later that morning. Father Ralph must decide whether to make known the new will, which leaves Mary's property to the Church instead of the Clearys but gives the Clearys financial security for life, or let stand Mary's old will, which left all her property to the Clearys as promised. Father Ralph, realizing that Mary's bequest will return him to the Church's good graces and clear the path for him to realize his ambitions, chooses the new will, cheating his beloved Meggie and her family of their rightful inheritance.<br /><br />Prior to Mary's party, Frank had, after a confrontation with Paddy in which it is revealed that Paddy is not Frank's biological father, run off with a troop of traveling professional boxers. After the party, Ralph leaves the Outback and moves up the Church hierarchy. Paddy and Meggie's favorite remaining brother Stu meet a gruesome death in a bush fire. This leaves Meggie emotionally alone at Drogheda with her aloof mother and her surviving brothers always off working the vast sheep station. Luke O'Neill comes to Drogheda to work as a stockman (more or less a cowboy in American terms).  Meggie marries Luke because he somewhat resembles Father Ralph. Luke, a miserly man, only wants Meggie's money. Luke takes Meggie to Queensland and takes control of Meggie's money, then hires Meggie out as a servant to a kindly couple while he goes off to travel the country working with a gang of sugar cane cutters. Luke tells Meggie he is saving to buy them their own sheep station in Queensland. Meggie bears Luke a daughter she names Justine, but Luke doesn't care. As Meggie realizes that Luke loves having money in the bank and has no intent of making a home with her, the marriage grows worse.<br /><br />The couple for whom Meggie works arrange for Meggie to go off by herself to a honeymoon resort without Justine so she can think about her future. When Father Ralph comes looking for Meggie, the friends send him on to the resort, where Father Ralph and Meggie consummate their passion.  After Father Ralph leaves, Meggie knows she is pregnant. She returns to Luke and sleeps with him one more time to alleviate suspicion that the child could be Father Ralph's. Then she tells Luke she is leaving him permanently and going home to Drogheda. Because Luke resembles Father Ralph superficially, most people, including Father Ralph, never guess that the son Meggie bears, Dane, is Father Ralph's child.  Meggie remains at Drogheda, refusing all potential suitors. She holds to her memories of her few days with Father Ralph, and lives out her days with her aging mother and her brothers who never marry.<br /><br />From this point the book meanders. A lengthy chapter covers the adventures of Meggie's younger twin brothers in World War II. Fiona tells Meggie that she knowsDane is Father Ralph's child, and Meggie and her mother become closer. We learn that Frank (who has, while offstage, gone to jail for murder) is the son of a married Maori politician with whom Fiona was in love as a teenager.<br /><br />Justine rebels from her time in the womb and directs irrational hostility at Meggie and Father Ralph (who remains a family friend while his career takes him up to the level of Cardinal and a post at the Vatican in Rome). Dane adores Meggie and Father Ralph and is near-saintly. Justine goes off to Sydney and then London to pursue an acting career. Dane enters a seminary in Rome, under Father Ralph's sponsorship, to become a priest. A German friend of Father Ralph's, Rainer, becomes enamored of the prickly Justine when she comes to visit Dane in Rome, and pursues her carefully for years. Father Ralph intervenes and gets Frank out of jail after 30 years. Dane dies shortly after his ordination, after rescuing some people who were in danger of drowning. Dane's body is brought back to Drogheda for burial, and Meggie finally tells Father Ralph that Dane was his son. Father Ralph dies in Meggie's arms at Drogheda. In London, Justine marries Rainer.<br /><br />Sorry, that was a lot of plot talk, but I think it serves to illustrate what's right and wrong with \\"The Thorn Birds.\\"<br /><br />Up until Meggie leaves Luke and returns to Drogheda, the story is for the most part engaging and thematically consistent. Author Colleen McCullough has her strengths. She creates a wonderful sense of place. Her descriptions of the lushly verdant, gentle New Zealand and daily life in  bitterly harsh Australia are unforgettable, if somewhat overlong at times. Her powerful writing occasionally attains elegance. The stories from Meggie's early childhood hook the reader on the characters and make us what to know about their pasts and their futures. She creates intriguing and/or sympathetic characters in Paddy, Fiona, Frank, Meggie and even the single-minded, selfish Luke. Mary Carson is an entertaining spider of a deus ex machina. Some people fault Meggie for being bland, but I think she's actually rather believable even though many women today would tell her, \\"Get over it.\\" Meggie has not been brought up to think she deserves or should expect much out of life. She describes herself as an average woman who wants average things: a husband, a home, children. Following her mother's stoic example, she takes the crumbs she gets with little complaint. Her ordinariness is, I suspect, a big reason \\"The Thorn Birds\\" was so wildly popular in its day. The slow softening of Fiona's armor and the revelation of her past is fascinating. The book raises some interesting questions about the nature of faith, and why some men are called to make the sacrifice of manhood that the Catholic priesthood demands. In light of today's scandals about the Church moving child-molesting priests to other locations rather than treating the problem or removing them from the priesthood, the episode in which Father Ralph disciplines a priest who has got himself entangled in an affair with a woman by shipping him off to the remotest part of Australia is telling--just send the problem priest elsewhere.<br /><br />Unfortunately, \\"The Thorn Birds\\" also has some gaping problems. Too many questions are left unanswered about the other central character, Father Ralph. What did he do that so insulted the bishop that his career was derailed into the Outback. More important, why is he so ruthlessly ambitious for advancement within the Church? If, as he says, he feels like a holy vessel, why does he need to be a cardinal? Couldn't he serve just as well as a parish priest?<br /><br />There is something undisciplined about the whole undertaking. Does McCullough want to educate us about life on outback sheep stations and in Queensland sugar cane country, or tell a story about her characters? We read dozens of pages about distances, gates, weather, dust, flies, birds, plants,sheep, and the smell of sugar processing plants. Some of that effectively sets scenes and illuminates the toughness of these people, but it gets boring after a while. Why do we spend all that time off with Meggie's younger twin brothers in World War II? Why does Meggie have so many brothers anyway? Other than Frank, the gentle Stu, and Hal who dies as an infant, it's hard to tell one from the other. Why do we spend so much time on the career and love life of the unpleasant Justine?  Why do we spend so much time getting to know Frank, and then he is, in a manner of speaking, kicked out of the book? The episode in which Paddy and Stu die and their bodies are dragged through the mud back to Drogheda goes on and on in gory, sticky, slippery, sweaty detail and seems unrelated to the theme of people giving their lives, through death or living death, for a moment of perfect joy. Meggie endures too much pointless tragedy for credence: her rowdy brothers destroy the beautiful doll she receives for her fourth birthday; her father runs her best school friend out of town after the girl gives Meggie head lice; Meggie loves a man she can't have, marries a man who could care less whether she's happy, and loses the men who do love her--her father, Frank, Stu and Dane. Little effort is made to have the characters speak in different voices.<br /><br />I don't mind that this book does not have a happy ending. Romance novels with happy endings are a dime a dozen. Romance novels with unhappy or ambivalent endings seem to endure--look at \\"Gone with the Wind\\" or all those \\"fallen woman\\" classics like \\"Madame Bovary\\" and \\"Anna Karenina.\\"  But I do think \\"The Thorn Birds\\" has the wrong ending. It would have been better had it been restructured to reveal Fiona's past earlier, to flesh out more of what happened to Frank, to eliminate all the material about Meggie's children's adult lives, and to end when Meggie returns to Drogheda for good. \\"The Thorn Birds\\" is more than OK, so I gave it 4 stars. With McCullough's raw talent as a writer and storyteller, it should, however, have been better.

B005CM9DX6|2.0|It's not metal, it's technically not 'progressive'...so...what is it? The only element inherent within this album that appears to warrant a 'prog rock' tag is the obvious King Crimson influence (among others I'm sure) as evidenced by the jazz-centric riffs, mellotrons, and random flute solos. However, where King Crimson was innovative and insightful, truly a breath of fresh air, \\"Heritage\\" sounds like a band attempting to emulate the haunting beauty of their prog rock predecessors, but falling way short of the mark.<br /><br />The more listens I give \\"Heritage\\" the more the nonsensical repetition and soulless meandering irritates me. Upon first listen I thought the album was great (4 stars perhaps) and expected the listening experience to exponentially improve, but instead found that greater attention to detail revealed many weaknesses. The compositions are tepid, aimless affairs and the entire album plods along at a snail's pace. The 'progression' feels forced and uninspired, as if Akerfeldt is trying way too hard to do something different for prog rock's sake. An interesting exotic instrument will occasionally make an appearance, but kick around just long enough to remind you of its presence and then depart, barring any sense of inspiration or purpose. Additionally, I was hoping the lyrics would at least be thought provoking, but unfortunately, no respite; from 'Folklore': \\"Hey you, will you be true/When you can see through what I do/Feel the pain in your brain, insane\\". I sincerely wish the rest of the album stuck with that formula. 'The Devil's Orchard' incorporates a nice jazzy drum riff and syncopated rhythm; a stand out track. On the other hand, 'Marrow Of The Earth' is the definition of disposable like many songs on this album; nothing interesting or conclusive, just filler. Honestly, the entire album could be mistaken as a laborious jam session recording.<br /><br />Overall, \\"Heritage\\" is a self-serving album that consists of exactly the kind of material Akerfeldt presumably wanted to make, which is admirable, yet will inevitably end up splitting Opeth's loyal fanbase. I admire artists who don't pander to an audience and seek to please themselves first, however, I fear this album will end up satisfying very few. It's not particularly enjoyable from either a technical or pure enjoyment perspective (for me at least), but it could be worse. Needless to say, this one won't be staying in my collection for much longer. Alternatively, Arch/Matheos' \\"Sympathetic Resonance\\" is an excellent example of progressive metal that utilizes complex, intellectually and creatively stimulating compositions without relying on the old exotic instrumentation crutch; highly recommended.s a nice jazzy drum riff and syncopated rhythm; a stand out track. B004G6009K|3.0|The story of Rapunzel is one of my favorite Grimms' fairy tale. The original story and many of its variations are quite dark. In the original story the prince calls for Rapunzel to let down her hair and sees her on the sly. One day she lets slip that her dress is getting too tight around her waist. (She's pregnant.) Gothel, the woman who kidnapped Rapunzel, traps the prince when he returns. He falls from the tower and lands in thorns, either blinding him or killing him outright depending on which cultural interpretation you read.<br /><br />Now I don't expect Disney to tell a story like that. It is Disney after all and the lost princess must remain virginal - while at the same time having an unusually close relationship with the animals around her. And, yes, the guy she loves in Tangled undergoes a life-changing (literally!) experience in the end. But for all that, I didn't like this movie as much. And I wanted to like it because as a writer I see a lot of potential in the basic story of Rapunzel. Rapunzel empowered<br /><br />First, this movie came apart for me at the level of the story. There were good parts to it, but overall the story didn't do it for me. I liked many elements but once again I felt as if I was watching another formulaic effort from Disney. Captured/lonely princess. Check. Evil grandmother/stepmother. Check. Funny and helpful animals. Check.  Handsome single man comes into her life. Check. Beautiful background and story boardinng. Check. But there has to be more than that. We've seen all this before. There has to be a story that grabs us. We're not all eleven year old girls, Disney. Come on. Get out of the rut.<br /><br />Perfect example. In the beginning of the movie Rapunzel's mother is ill and needs a magic plant to heal her so Rapunzel can be born. But in the original fairy tale the mother needs lettuce (sometimes it's parsley) and the king makes a deal with Gothel in which he hands over the baby to save the life of the queen. Now that's pretty dark right there. Even if he is motivated by saving the life of his queen, and must be forced to make that choice, Disney doesn't tell it that way. In this retelling, Gothel steals the baby from its crib. We lose whole subterranean plots and motivations in this one move. Gothel kidnaps the baby because she's evil and greedy. It's Disney. Of course she is evil and greedy.<br /><br />Finally, for the prince, in which this case it's a lovable thief, well, he serves the same function as all other love interests in Disney animated films. He's a plot point. Albeit a funny one in this case, and one in which Disney worked to flesh out -- but still a plot point. After all, Rapunzel has to eventually give her virginity to somebody, and it's not going to be that chameleon that rides her shoulder or Maximus the palace horse. Thus, we have the prince/thief/substitute-male figure who comes into her life in order to round out the story.<br /><br />As far as the character of Rapunzel herself, I did like her a lot. She went through the motions we expect her to and we're on her side. The sight gags of her doing things with her hair were funny and the running gag with the frying pan was cute. We like her and we want her to win her freedom and her love in the end. But the story that surrounds her doesn't click. It's not the story that allows her to find true happiness, it's Disney that makes it come true. Just like always. And so the movie loses a lot of its impact right there because we are never frightened for Rapunzel. Come to think of it, we are never frightened by any Disney movies anymore. Which was not always the case. Disney used to have a reputation of showing some fairly dark things on screen. Or at least stories that demanded, and gave, emotional investment and experience.<br /><br />We don't get that at all with Tangled.<br /><br />Part of the problem, I think, is the animation. Sometimes it is absolutely gorgeous...and sometimes we are taken aback by how bad it looks and the jerks and stopRapunzel gets tangled in Tangleds between scenes. To be sure there are many nice scenes (from an artistic perspective) in this film. The scene where the dam bursts, the dance in the market square, the flight of the paper lanterns all stand out. The clothing and texture of trees and stone and water are phenomenal. But the characters themselves...well, sometimes they look kind of rubbery. Like walking dolls made out of wax. I was disappointed in that. If you're going to go the limit to make clothing have proper texture, and move correctly upon the frame, then why not make the skin and shading on the characters more realistic as well?<br /><br />The music was another big let down. None of the songs were memorable. Even Rapunzel's healing song doesn't stick with you, and that song, more than any other, ties the arc of the film together. Most of the humor works and some falls flat. But that's Disney and that's their trademark and they're not going to let it go no matter how much we groan. At least we see some effort at background and characterization for many of the secondary characters. That helps round out many of the other flaws.<br /><br />Not surprisingly, the one character that really stands out (and takes the stage away from Rapunzel when they're together) is Gothel. This is where Disney shines.  If there's one thing Disney knows it's \\"evil stepmother\\" and how to represent her as a multi-facted and three-dimensional character who is cruel, cunning, and manipulative. As much as I like Rapunzel, and the idea of story potential about Rapunzel, as a professional writer I am here to tell you that a story about Gothel is much more interesting. But, once again, we are limited by Disney and the parameters they set up for every film they make. We will never see a story about Gothel, or Rapunzel for that matter, the way it should be told from Disney. It's not what they do.<br /><br />As I said earlier I really wanted to like this film. But there's too much missing or awkward about it, or worse yet, formulaic. Tangled was billed as the 50th animated film by Disney. In all that time they have made groundbreaking strides in the technical department. But the story of Tangled is like every other Lonely Princess story we've seen from Disney and that, more than anything else, is why I can't recommend it.To be sure there are many nice scenes (from an artistic perspective) in this film. The scene where the dam bursts, the dance in the market square, the flight of the paper lanterns all stand out. The clothing and texture of trees and stone and water are phenomenal. But the characters themselves...well, sometimes they look kind of rubbery. Like walking dolls made out of wax. I was disappointed in that. If you're going to go the limit to make clothing have proper texture, and move correctly upon the frame, then why not make the skin and shading on the characters more realistic as well?  The music was another big let down. None of the songs were memorable. Even Rapunzel's healing song doesn't stick with you, and that song, more than any other, ties the arc of the film together. Most of the humor works and some falls flat. But that's Disney and that's their trademark and they're not going to let it go no matter how much we groan. At least we see some effort at background and characterization for many of the secondary characters. That helps round out many of the other flaws.  Not surprisingly, the one character that really stands out (and takes the stage away from Rapunzel when they're together) is Gothel. This is where Disney shines.  If there's one thing Disney knows it's \\"evil stepmother\\" and how to represent her as a multi-facted and three-dimensional character who is cruel, cunning, and manipulative. As much as I like Rapunzel, and the idea of story potential about Rapunzel, as a professional writer I am here to tell you that a story about Gothel is much more interesting. But, once again, we are limited by Disney and the parameters they set up for every film they make. We will never see a story about Gothel, or Rapunzel for that matter, the way it should be told from Disney. It's not what they do.  As I said earlier I really wanted to like this film. But there's too much missing or awkward about it, or worse yet, formulaic. Tangled was billed as the 50th animated film by Disney. In all that time they have made groundbreaking strides in the technical department. But the story of Tangled is like every other Lonely Princess story we've seen from Disney and that, more than anything else, is why I can't recommend it.

B003E6K1VK|5.0|Headphones are not meant to be run from crummy jacks.  I realize most people would never consider this, but considering how ubiquitous headphone listening has become, it's an essential fact we should share with others.  Furthermore, most good headphone jacks aren't even designed with the ability to properly drive the majority of headphones that are low impedance.  That's startling.<br /><br />I'm astonished at the measurements this headphone amp just produced through RMAA and how good the waveforms look in TrueRTA.  At the price this is going for, I recommend the E7 for anyone with good headphones.  Everyone.  If you own a good pair of headphones (or more than one pair), buy one.  Used, new, refurbished.  Just buy one.  Heck, it even makes cheap bundled earbuds sound better, which tend to suffer from severe bass roll-off (even more than is inherent to them) and unnatural highs directly from most laptops, home receivers, and portable players.  As bad as those earbuds are, it's curious how improved they are with this.<br /><br />Aside from its limited maximum output into low sensitivities AND high impedances, its performance is as-good or better than the Grado RA1, and you get a somewhat decent USB DAC and two whole separate headphone amps in the E7.  The digital volume control also has no significant channel imbalance at ANY SETTING.  Fantastic.  Like most headphone amps that have AC/USB and battery options, its performance is slightly better on battery power.  Both the jacks on top are independent, with only a slight reduction in some performance as they share a common power supply section.  That reduction in performance when both jacks are used is mostly less than when you're on USB power, by the way.  Even on USB power and with both jacks in use (which might be a good idea for some headphone pairs since the batteries can only supply so much current & swing at a time at high volumes), the performance still beats any other mass-produced competitor at this price level... and that's just comparing the amp section.  Seems like a no-brainer.<br /><br />The analog input apparently wants Pro Nominal (1.25V) or less to perform optimally, which is probably above 50% on Windows volume control with the Emu 0204 line output that is itself a little over 2V at 0dBFS, but I haven't tried boosting it in tiny increments to determine what the best level is yet.  82% on the Windows volume is the maximum for me to prevent overt distortion with a normalized signal, but the E7 surprisingly soft-distorts in the areas between 82% and 50% (1.25V is somewhere right above 50%, I think).  That's really good distortion characteristics for the aux analog input if you do accidentally go over Pro Nominal.  Try not to, but it's interesting to note it's a bit forgiving in that regard.  You still get significantly better measurements at or below Pro Nominal, it just doesn't distort quickly or terribly like, say, my old Biamp DJ mixers did when pushed marginally beyond that.<br /><br />This gets to my one criticism: the firmware could use an upgrade to add an input meter or clipping indicator.<br /><br />When using the USB DAC, just put the Windows volume control at max (if it's not bypassed automatically like on mine) and make sure the program you're using doesn't clip in the digital domain.  The non-ASIO 16bit/44.1-48khz Wolfson DAC in the E7 is not the last degree of detail or refinement and is maybe a touch warm over neutral, but it's got welcome intimations of the ever-mysterious PRaT and does the job better than those on most (probably nearly all) built into laptops and cheap sound cards.  It's a distinct and welcome difference from the Emu for some content and headphones in spite of its crudeness and lack of pedigree.  Hey, it's a sub-$100 amp & DAC combo.  Think of it as a bonus.  Since few laptops have dedicated line outputs, using the USB DAC does become a requirement to get the most out of the E7 amplifier if you do not have a better DAC, as otherwise you will be limited to the performance of not only the laptop's DAC, but the headphone amp in there, too.<br /><br />The E7 can drive any impedance headphone properly, even the lowest impedance ones.  If you need more volume on very insensitive AND high impedance headphones, the E9 (or E7 + E9 docked combo if you want to use the Wolfson DAC) is a solution for more brute output at a reduced performance.  Start with the E7, though.  It is true that for lower sensitivity and/or high impedance headphones at a higher price point, the playing field opens up and the E9, though competitive, isn't the automatic option.  And do not buy or use the E9 for low impedance headphones, as it's not designed for that.  If you have one of those expensive, hard-drive-based mega-capacity iPods in a similar form factor, then an E7 and an iPod line-out to 3.5mm dongle could be the easiest purchase decision you've ever made.only the laptop's DAC, but the headphone amp in there, too. 0307887898|5.0|I regard this book as a great lesson in SPEED and ACCURACY - two things essential to the success of startup or anyone who wants to get their products in the hands of customers. [[ASIN:0307887898 The Lean Startup: How Today's Entrepreneurs Use Continuous Innovation to Create Radically Successful Businesses]] is the antidote to wasted time, wasted money and useless innovation (products and services no one wants.) For most entrepreneurs, including myself, we follow our instincts, become very attached to our ideas, or when we finally test our value proposition customers feedback is disappointing because they aren't as excited or receptive as we would like them to be. If you want them PAYING CASH and funding your product development this book is worth the read.  Think about it; everyone knows, or thinks they know what people want. Right? But you don't.  Think about the last few birthday gifts you received.  How many of them did you really want and how many of them are gathering dust or returned?  At its core this book is about market testing and although I've done marketing research and testing many times Ries's approach with the Minimum Viable Product, (MVP) and Validated Learning takes market tests to a level. You may have ideas about \\"what\\" and \\"who\\" and \\"where\\" you want to innovate, but before you build that concept Reis advocated you break it down into its smallest testable products and then get customer feedback from your targets. If testing your MVP means going face to face, door to door to get feedback from 15 to 20 people - that is much cheaper and faster than building an expensive prototype with all the bells and whistles.  Its become very difficult to find out what the customer didn't want if you already build in 10 features a product with everything you \\"think\\" the market wants. Speed to test is the key and in the process that will give you the data needed inexpensively to know what to build and for idea 2.0.  If you are testing your assumptions (at first) and measuring the right metrics, your innovation will move through a scientific process that Reis calls validated learning. Validated Learning is the other major concept discussed in this book and if you follow this process you'll avoid fooling yourself with what he calls Vanity Metrics.   Actionable Metrics are measures of real customer behavior and drive the scientific process to support Innovation Accounting, not to be confused with standard accounting.     The AUDACITY of ZERO - I have to say I thought this was a great way to explain this concept - Big resources and big numbers can be deceiving because of how and what gets measured; too many illogical connections can be made and vanity or just plan self-deception can cloud judgments about the new innovation.  Having SMALL numbers to work with (few prospects, customers and sales) can pour cold water on an innovation and if you are not measuring the right things could be bogus too. If there isn't enough hard evidence to support what works and doesn't work because you are only looking at prospect v sales you might not be looking at the right thing, yet. Each innovation, whether it starts in an established company or a basement workbench, starts at zero. Zero is a great place to be because you can test each assumption and test each hypothesis along the way for the true measure of value to the customer - it's completely honest; there is nothing to contaminate the data if you have clearly defined your hypothesis and measured your results scientifically.  I think there are two concepts that need to be acknowledged here:  The first is the concept of incremental improvement, grandfathered by Dr. W. Edwards Deming a consultant and statistician the United States sent to help rebuild post WWII Japan. He fathered, documented and implemented the idea and the Japanese took it on and made it their business and cultural philosophy known as Kaizen [[ASIN:0761129235 One Small Step Can Change Your Life: The Kaizen Way]].  Reis is obviously a believer as one of his favorite references is Toyota and how they have become the world largest automaker, largely by small, incremental steps.  The second concept that Reis acknowledges is his idea of market testing, i.e. MVP and Validated Learning came from Direct Response Marketers and A/B testing [[ASIN:1440511845 The Ultimate Marketing Plan: Target Your Audience! Get Out Your Message! Build Your Brand!]]. This has been around for a long time, but I believe no one has put it into a context as simple as Reis has in [[ASIN:0307887898 The Lean Startup: How Today's Entrepreneurs Use Continuous Innovation to Create Radically Successful Businesses]].   The short and sweet of this book is that if an entrepreneur simply throws out his or her idea to see if it works or not she will succeed. Because she will be proven right, it either worked or it failed. Success, right? But that isn't really the goal is it? An entrepreneur wants their product to succeed, fill a need and make a profit. Testing Minimum Viable Products (MVP)s and measuring results against predetermined assumptions will quickly guide you to what sells, to whom, at what price, how it should be sold and delivered. Minimizing build-out time and speeding up your profits.

0545091020|4.0|Suzanne Collins tells us that The Hunger Games trilogy was inspired by twin causes in her life: the pain of living while her father fought in the Vietnam War, and the Classical story of Theseus and the Minotaur, which resonated deeply with her psyche. This makes sense. My first impression, however, was I was reading an episode of the TV reality series Survivor, cross-bred with Golding's Lord of the Flies.<br /><br />I was about 20 pages in when I rolled my eyes and thought, \\"Great. Not another dystopian novel.\\" I'm starting to miss fantasy and science fiction where we get a glimpse of a good future. Recently, I've read Gibson's Neuromancer and Bacigalupi's The Windup Girl. I thought I'd just about had enough of icky post-apocalyptic futures, thank you very much. But I trudged on.<br /><br />Imagine a future where a rich city-state (\\"The Capitol\\") controls the thirteen districts that once comprised the United States. The story's Heroine, sixteen-year-old Katniss, is from district twelve (formerly the Appalachian range), where the populace is forced to mine coal. A thirteenth district was obliterated by The Capitol in the years that led to its dominance.<br /><br />As a form of punishment, the capitol puts every child between the ages of 12-18 into a lottery, where one boy and one girl from each district might be selected to compete in The Hunger Games. These games are really a death match between the 24 combattants; the last child standing is crowned the Victor. This is a form of punishment from the Capitol, to tell each district that they can, and will, do anything they like. The populace of each district are subjected to nonstop slave labor. The Capitol can, and will, do anything to dissentors--up to, and including, killing their children. Some premise, huh? You are sure to guess what happens: Katniss is ultimately selected to be the female champion from District Twelve.<br /><br />So far, I don't sound like I enjoyed the book much. Don't get me wrong: I did. I liked the story, and the love interest with the male champion from Twelve (his name was Peeta) was just innocent enough to captivate me. Katniss's wonder at the extravagance and excess of The Capitol was enjoyable, as well as the series of small victories that led her to be crowned Victor. (You didn't think she'd actually lose, did you?)<br /><br />Still, a few things detracted from the story. Katniss seems to go into times of desparation that pass \\"in a minute, or an hour, or a month.\\" Collins's description of the heroine's food was not-quite-erotic, full of \\"plump berries bursting juice into onto my eager tongue\\" and distracting. In general, thought, the writing was solid, if a bit melodramatic. Thematically, the novel seems to beat a tired drumskin: a plucky arrow-shootin' girl makes good, and shows the whole country what she's made of. It's a good, but not a great, exemplar of this plot. Still, I admit, it's a lot of fun.<br /><br />I saw that some people compared it thematically to Macbeth. Yeah. Right. I recently read where author Jim Butcher said \\"[writing comic books] is to novel writing as monosodium glutamate is to classical opera.\\" By no means is The Hunger Games simply a comic book. Neither, I think, should it ever, ever, be misconstrued as on the same par with Macbeth.<br /><br />I realize the third novel of The Hunger Games trilogy was just released. I have read it, and will review it soon. These things take time.  For now, suffice it to say that the Hunger Games was an decent, if a bit imperfect, start to a good story. Soon, let you know what I think of the rest of the series.<br /><br />Four of Five Stars.the male champion from Twelve (his name was Peeta) was just innocent enough to captivate me. There is never any part where the reader's emotions are allowed to rest. Like Katniss, I was always on edge, waiting for her next challenge, waiting for the next bad thing to happen. This makes it a fast read. Also, since it's told through Katniss's eyes and the storytelling is excellent, I was completely involved in the story from start to finish.<br /><br />Haymitch is by far my favorite character, with Katniss being a close second. I thought all the characters were wonderfully done. I got a sense of each of them without being told exactly who they were, what they did, and what I should think of them. I think this envelops all of the book, actually. She doesn't dumb down the writing just because it's young adult. Problems don't magically disappear and there isn't any forced happily-ever-afters. It's violent, brutal, and dark. Those who are squeamish will have a hard time with this story, but I think it's worth it. For one thing, the violence isn't there for shock value, as is the case for some novels, unfortunately. Rather, it's a statement about the government of Panem and what these people are forced to live with. I like that the characters are forced to work through their problems and actually deal with issues. It makes for an interesting story, the characters are allowed to grow and change, and it reflects the real world. 0439023483|5.0|The Hunger Games opens with a bleak picture of our future. After some North American catastrophe that is merely hinted at, we are introduced to the nation of Panem, a glimmering but austere Capitol surrounded by twelve specialized and somewhat downtrodden Districts. Our heroine, Katniss Everdeen, hails from the coal mining District 12, where she and her friend Gale must hunt in the forests (illegally) for food and supplies their families wouldn't be able to afford otherwise. At sixteen, Katniss has spent most of her adolescence signed up for the Hunger Games, where two children conscripted from each District fight to the death for the honor of bringing home wealth, food and prestige. When Katniss' little sister's name is drawn for this years Games, Katniss volunteers in her stead. What happens next goes far beyond the needs of Katniss' family and opens up a greater world of danger, intrigue, romance and adventure.<br /><br />Suzanne Collins clearly has a plan that extends beyond this book. As the first part of a trilogy, The Hunger Games must set up the characters, locations, events and themes to service the entire overall story. However, at no point does the book feel dry or overly expository. The perspective of Katniss both allows for the introduction of the necessary elements mentioned and keeps us firmly in the narrative of the story at hand. It's a fantastic example of characterization and plotting woven together to create a coherent first act that manages to stand alone.<br /><br />Speaking of characters, Collins also does a wonderful job fleshing out the people of Panem. Katniss as a heroine is at once strong and vulnerable, intelligent and naive. She feels, talks and reacts like a real person, with palpable confusion in some moments and grim resolution in others. Her fellow tribute from District 12, Peeta, shows a great deal of complexity as well, along with some of the adults involved and the tributes from other Districts. The entire enterprise from start to finish has all of the hallmarks of careful construction, not only creating this new world of a potential future but also giving readers a reason to care about it.<br /><br />The sensationalism and spin doctoring of Panem surrounding the Hunger Games and the undercurrent of oppression and misery feels close to home. There are eerie similarities between the ways in which the Capitol interacts with its Districts and the rhetoric and attitude of certain elements in today's world in general and the United States in particular. Between this similarity and the presentation of Katniss, Collins draws the reader in and refuses to let go, compelling each page to turn as the action unfolds. When the book is over, the readers is satisfied with the conclusion but left wanting more, which is exactly how any book should end, but especially when more are planned to come after it.<br /><br />The Hunger Games is a wonderful book, deeply involving and a delight to read. And yet it's only the first part of a greater narrative exercise.  Subsequent books are poised to deliver more great characterization, a deeper exploration of the world of Panem, and more sleepless nights for the reader as they (that is, we) eagerly turn page after page.  Good luck putting this one down.the hallmarks of careful construction, not only creating this new world of a potential future but also giving readers a reason to care about it.  The sensationalism and spin doctoring of Panem surrounding the Hunger Games and the undercurrent of oppression and misery feels close to home. There are eerie similarities between the ways in which the Capitol interacts with its Districts and the rhetoric and attitude of certain elements in today's world in general and the United States in particular. Between this similarity and the presentation of Katniss, Collins draws the reader in and refuses to let go, compelling each page to turn as the action unfolds. When the book is over, the readers is satisfied with the conclusion but left wanting more, which is exactly how any book should end, but especially when more are planned to come after it.  The Hunger Games is a wonderful book, deeply involving and a delight to read. And yet it's only the first part of a greater narrative exercise.  Subsequent books are poised to deliver more great characterization, a deeper exploration of the world of Panem, and more sleepless nights for the reader as they (that is, we) eagerly turn page after page.  Good luck putting this one down.

B004XIQIPQ|4.0|Arctic Monkeys had an unusual rise to the top of the charts for a band in the 21st century. Lead singer Alex Turner and guitarist Jamie Cook started Arctic Monkeys along with Matt Helders on drums and bassist Andy Nicholson; all students at the same high school. After playing a series of shows in their hometown, they began to distribute demos on the Internet and their fan base began to grow at a rate worthy of major record label attention. Instead of jumping into a contract with one of the biggest record companies, however, Arctic Monkeys opted to sign with Domino Records in 2005, the same as Franz Ferdinand, a band to which Arctic Monkeys received many early comparisons.<br /><br />Like Franz Ferdinand, the expectations for Arctic Monkeys' debut album were extremely high, but the band proved easily worthy of the hype when \\"Whatever People Say I Am, That's What I'm Not\\" hit the shelves in 2006. Although the album received a moderate amount of attention in the United States, selling over 300,000 copies to date, \\"Whatever People Say I Am...\\" became the fastest selling record in U.K. history, selling over 360,000 copies in the first week alone with well over one million to date.<br /><br />After a second album, \\"Favourite Worst Nightmare,\\" received glowing praise on par with their debut, Arctic Monkeys began to change directions with their sound. Producer Josh Homme from Queens of the Stone Age offered his expertise, which built up some additional anticipation in the U.S., but the resulting third full-length release, \\"Humbug,\\" still fell to mixed reviews. Arctic Monkeys' music became darker and a bit more laid back than their preceding efforts, but with third albums notoriously difficult for most bands in the limelight, many fans quickly began speculating as to whether Arctic Monkeys would be able to bounce back.<br /><br />Now, two years later, Arctic Monkeys' fourth release puts all of that speculation to rest. Opening with \\"She's Thunderstorms,\\" it's clear the band has learned a great deal from their time with Josh Homme, but is still unwilling to just return to the proven formula of their first two albums. Still less energetic than early hits like \\"I Bet You Look Good on the Dance Floor\\" and \\"When the Sun Goes Down,\\" \\"She's Thunderstorms\\" finds Alex Turner singing more like a lounge singer than a teenage punk rocker, but the lyrics are equally clever which is what drew many fans to the band in the first place.<br /><br />Although \\"Suck It and See\\" offers many memorable and frequently amusing moments thanks to Alex Turner's wordplay, the title track, found one song before the album comes to a close, offers some of his finest work with each verse offering a unique blend of imagery and emotion. \\"You're rarer than a can of dandelion and burdock and other girls are just postmix lemonade\\" caps off the first descriptive verse while \\"that's not a skirt, girl, that's a sawn-off shotgun and I can only hope you've got it aimed at me\\" furthers his sentiments on the next.<br /><br />The first track heard by most fans on \\"Suck It and See\\" was \\"Brick By Brick,\\" which was released as a video on March 10. With a chorus more reminiscent of The Vines than any of their own past singles, \\"Brick By Brick\\" is still a retreat from their first two records, but where \\"Humbug\\" often suffered from a lack of their original energy, \\"Brick By Brick\\" is a rediscovery of that forgotten vigor with a classic twist. Instead of relying on speed, the Arctic Monkeys and producer James Ford utilized live takes to capture the chemistry between band members. The resulting music is every bit as engaging as their first two albums, but ends up sounding more authentic.<br /><br />With the sharp bass grooves of \\"Reckless Serenade\\" and the atmospheric guitar effects heard on tracks like \\"Piledriver Waltz\\" and \\"Love is a Laserquest\\" filling out the album with different textures, \\"Suck It and See\\" offers plenty of variety, but there is still a natural flow between songs. Fans of the original Arctic Monkeys' sound will gravitate to the fast paced verses of \\"Library Pictures,\\" but despite fitting the mold that made them famous it's the only track that feels slightly out of place.<br /><br />\\"Suck It and See\\" is everything most fans hoped \\"Humbug\\" would be. As a fan of Arctic Monkeys from the beginning, it is difficult to hear the band evolve without reminiscing back to the beginning, but change is not always a bad thing. Alex Turner's lyrics still shine as brightly as ever and although Josh Homme is no longer the key producer, his influence is not forgotten. \\"Suck It and See\\" may not become a record breaker, but for any fans who lost some of their interest after \\"Humbug,\\" I encourage you to buy this album and see what Arctic Monkeys have become.<br /><br />Similar Artists: Franz Ferdinand, The Libertines, Kaiser Chiefs<br />Track Suggestion: \\"Don't Sit Down 'cause I've Moved Your Chair\\"e fast paced verses of \\"Library Pictures,\\" but despite fitting the mold that made them famous it's the only track that feels slightly out of place.  \\"Suck It and See\\" is everything most fans hoped \\"Humbug\\" would be. B0054N73EY|4.0|It's been a hot minute since Red Hot Chili Peppers guitarist John Frusciante left the band. By that, I mean he has been a part of every Red Hot Chili Peppers album since 1989 with the exception of 1995's \\"One Hot Minute\\" on which Jane's Addiction guitarist Dave Naverro took over.<br /><br />Following a 16-month tour in support of 2006's \\"Stadium Arcadium,\\" Red Hot Chili Peppers' epic two-disc set of new studio material, the band announced an indefinite hiatus to recover from their seemingly endless work since 1999's \\"Californication.\\" When their time apart finally came to an end in October of 2009, Frusciante was once again not a part of the early sessions and his departure from the band was made official a few months later.<br /><br />Replacing Frusciante is Josh Klinghoffer who has worked with the likes of Gnarls Barkley, PJ Harvey, Beck, and Warpaint among many others in the past. Instead of letting him show off his skills right off the bat, however, the opening track, \\"Monarchy of Roses\\" introduces Klinghoffer with screeches reminiscent of a young garage band's first rehearsal. Anthony Kiedis's vocals enter early in an uncharacteristically distorted fashion while drummer Chad Smith's low rumble of kick drum and toms drives the song forward. At first, longtime fans of Red Hot Chili Peppers may think Klinghoffer has forced the band in a completely new direction, but suddenly Smith drops a loud downbeat on his snare drum and the Red Hot Chili Peppers we've all become accustomed to hearing are back.<br /><br />As \\"I'm With You\\" begins to take shape, one thing becomes clear; this is a bass-heavy record. Original bassist Flea, who is widely considered one of the most talented bassists in the world, answers the changing of the guard on the guitarist side of the spectrum with some of his most involved parts to date. Sure, \\"Factory of Faith\\" and \\"Ethiopia\\" don't exactly have the same intensity or skill requirements of the bass line in \\"Coffee Shop,\\" one of the most impressive parts of \\"One Hot Minute,\\" but his importance to each track is felt more in the studio now than it ever was 20 years ago. Instead of utilizing the slap bass technique to rough up the edges of the mix, Flea's bass now has a much smoother quality to it. Switching back and forth between octaves, Flea leads the way in providing the funk styling that has frequently been the job of the guitar on past records.<br /><br />The upbeat wildness of Red Hot Chili Peppers that defined them in the '80s still makes its presence known occasionally on \\"I'm with You.\\" On \\"Goodbye Hooray,\\" Flea unleashes a fierce breakdown very much like those of their earliest records, but the distorted slap bass is still traded in for a smooth, clearer quality. On the contrary, the polished solos of Frusciante, like those heard on \\"Scar Tissue,\\" are approached much differently by Klinghoffer. Tracks like \\"Look Around\\" give the guitar its moment in the spotlight, but clarity and technique are traded in for spontaneous creativity. These aren't solos that have been perfected over months of practice. Instead, Klinghoffer gives the listener a more in the moment interpretation of his instrument.<br /><br />Although each track manages to blend a trademarked formula with new twist, \\"I'm With You\\" still has trouble tying ideas together. Transitions between songs have never been the strongest aspect of Red Hot Chili Peppers' albums. One of the biggest criticisms of \\"Stadium Arcadium\\" was that many songs seemed to act as filler between singles and a common theme appeared to be non-existent. \\"I'm With You\\" is an improvement, partially thanks to the lack of a second disk, but the jumps in style and subject between \\"Happiness Loves Company,\\" \\"Police Station,\\" and \\"Even You Brutus?\\" still demonstrates just how diverse a single album of theirs can be.<br /><br />Like most Red Hot Chili Peppers records, \\"I'm With You\\" improves each time you listen to it. Yes, the change in guitarist provides a distinct contrastbetween this and their last two decades of music, but after letting the album play through a few times, it feels less like a new chapter and more like a page turn.<br /><br />Although they seem to be booked everywhere in the world except the U.S. for the remainder of 2011, the true Red Hot Chili Peppers experience comes in seeing them live. With a U.S. tour sure to be around the corner in 2012, \\"I'm With You\\" is an excellent way for any fan to pass the time until their tour bus rolls into town. If you're new to Red Hot Chili Peppers, this is an excellent place to start, but make sure you explore deeper into their catalog after you've given \\"I'm With You\\" time to digest.<br /><br />Similar Artists: Primus, Jane's Addiction<br />Track Suggestion: \\"The Adventures of Rain Dance Maggie\\"between this and their last two decades of music, but after letting the album play through a few times, it feels less like a new chapter and more like a page turn.<br /><br />Although they seem to be booked everywhere in the world except the U.S. for the remainder of 2011, the true Red Hot Chili Peppers experience comes in seeing them live. B0053FP1PQ|4.0|Better known in their home country of England than they are in the United States, The Kooks debut record, \\"Inside In/Inside Out\\" was released in October 2006 in the U.S., 10 months after its initial release in the U.K.<br /><br />With tracks like \\"Nave\\" and \\"She Moves in Her Own Way\\" receiving a decent amount of radio play on independent stations across the United States, The Kooks developed a decent following, but did little to set themselves apart from the vast sea of bands with a similar musical formula like Kaiser Chiefs and Arctic Monkeys.<br /><br />After their second record, 2008's \\"Konk,\\" was released, The Kooks ended up taking some time off, in part due to the departure of bassist Max Rafferty. Fortunately for The Kooks, this may have been what saved them. Around the same time of The Kooks' hiatus, most of their competition began to deviate from their established successful formulas into more experimental territory.<br /><br />Kaiser Chiefs began to focus more on danceable beats, Arctic Monkeys teamed up with Queens of the Stone Age's Josh Homme for a full makeover and seemingly countless British bands followed suit with new, uncharted paths.<br /><br />While these bands all attempt to regain the fans they lost during their walkabouts, The Kooks have finally returned with an album that does little to separate itself from their past efforts, much to the delight of fans. That is not to say there is nothing new on this record, however.<br /><br />Splitting the album in half is \\"Time Above the Earth,\\" a string quartet driven track tied to The Kooks only by lead singer Luke Pritchard's voice. The tune is definitely surprising when hidden in the folds of a generally upbeat rock record, but \\"Runaway\\" quickly brings listeners back into familiar territory and \\"Time Above the Earth\\" becomes a welcome transition instead of a confusing new direction.<br /><br />The opening tune, \\"Junk of the Heart\\" picks up right where \\"Konk\\" left off with a feel-good mix of acoustic and clean electric guitar, light drums, and Luke Pritchard's vocals. Pritchard sounds a bit more like Julian Casablancas of The Strokes on this record thanks to some extra effects added by Tony Hoffer, their longtime producer, but fans of either band will not likely complain.<br /><br />As \\"Junk of the Heart\\" progresses, The Kooks begin to add in little changes here and there to keep things fresh, but the changes are not nearly as jarring as those made by their contemporaries. \\"Taking Pictures of You\\" adds in a wave of synths and reversed guitar effects, but the song easily remains radio ready with the acoustic guitar and vocals passing through production unscathed.<br /><br />Even the album's closer, \\"Mr. Nice Guy,\\" finds a familiar comfort in Pritchard's voice while the backing instrumentation is given the freedom to wander into the realm of distorted guitars and piano.<br /><br />In the chorus of the title track, Pritchard sings \\"I wanna make you happy, I wanna make you feel alive.\\" This could just as easily be a message for fans of The Kooks as it is for the subject of the song. Instead of experimenting with the formula that made them famous, The Kooks embraced it and returned with an album for their fans. \\"Junk of the Heart\\" is rarely original, but it is consistently enjoyable. Isn't that why we listen to music?<br /><br />Similar Artists: The Strokes, Arctic Monkeys<br />Track Suggestion: \\"Junk of the Heart\\"s vocals. B004XE0P5E|5.0|Justin Vernon's debut album as Bon Iver, 2008's \\"For Emma, Forever Ago,\\" came in at #47 on my list of the best albums of the 21st century's first decade. When I gave the album a recent listen as a refresher before experiencing his self-titled follow up, I regretted not listing it even higher. For all of its musical simplicity, \\"For Emma, Forever Ago\\" is a complex album with lasting charm.<br /><br />Few songs have ever captured as much beauty as \\"Re: Stacks\\" yet Vernon's crystal clear falsetto and softly strummed acoustic guitar are the only two ingredients.<br /><br />After creating what is now regarded as one of the greatest \\"breakup albums\\" in the eyes of many, Vernon formed one of the most unlikely collaborations in music when he joined up with Kanye West to assist in the creation of \\"My Beautiful Dark Twisted Fantasy,\\" Kanye's game-changing 2010 release. Kanye's album instantly put Bon Iver back in the spotlight and reignited fans' demand for a new album from the more surprising than ever songwriter.<br /><br />\\"Bon Iver\\" opens with \\"Perth\\" which quickly introduces many stylistic changes while keeping Vernon's trademark vocals and unparalleled songwriting very much in focus.<br /><br />Likely to be mistaken as silence for listeners afraid to turn up the volume, \\"Perth\\" opens with a faint recording of a train crossing before a much louder and exceptionally polished electric guitar cuts through. As the song progresses, \\"Perth\\" sees the utilization of far more effects than customary for Bon Iver and eventually blossoms into a surprisingly percussive track with a lush instrumentation.<br /><br />This is not the same Bon Iver that ran off to a hunting cabin to record an album three years ago.<br /><br />With many of the songs named after locations, both real and imaginary, Vernon paints a brilliant picture of each setting musically with the assistance of Rob Moose's string arrangements and Colin Stetson's horn playing, which both play an important role throughout \\"Bon Iver.\\"<br /><br />In the case of \\"Lisbon, OH,\\" an instrumental, the listeners are forced to use their imaginations to find connections between the small town and each sound effect added to the mix.<br /><br />Although \\"Bon Iver\\" offers many changes and surprises when compared to his simplistic debut, perhaps Vernon's most surprising track, \\"Beth/Rest,\\" is used as the final number. With a synthesizer part reminiscent of any '80s ballad and vocals more closely resembling Peter Gabriel than Justin Vernon, \\"Beth/Rest\\" is a far more confusing closer than \\"Re: Stacks\\" was in 2008, but still manages to be just as engaging.<br /><br />Justin Vernon may have moved on from the heartache heard on \\"For Emma, Forever Ago,\\" but \\"Bon Iver\\" is still ripe with emotion and his songwriting is nearly as powerful now as it was then. Often when artists make such a drastic turn from their original sound, the result is disappointing, but Vernon's use of elaborate instrumentations and experimental effects do nothing to take away from the beauty of his music as a whole.<br /><br />\\"For Emma, Forever Ago\\" is still his crowning achievement, but \\"Bon Iver\\" could almost be considered a new beginning for him, making the self-titled name of the album a perfect choice.<br /><br />Similar Artists: Iron & Wine, Nick Drake<br /><br />Track Suggestion: \\"Calgary\\"listeners are forced to use their imaginations to find connections between the small town and each sound effect added to the mix. In this case, \\"Merlin\\" is based on the evolution of its four main characters: Merlin (Colin Morgan) as he grows from inexperienced youth to wise mentor, Arthur (Bradley James), who progresses from prattish prince to noble king, Morgana (Katie McGrath), whose fall into darkness provides the show's most tragic arc, and Guinevere (Angel Coulby), a young servant girl who will one day be Queen of Camelot.<br /><br />Rounding out the cast is Anthony Head as King Uther, an uncompromising ruler whose persecution of magic provides the main conflict of the show (for Merlin must keep his innate powers a secret at all costs) and Richard Wilson as Gaius the court physician, who lends support and advice to Merlin. It is these characters and the range of complex relationships they share, whether familial, romantic or antagonistic, that make the show so appealing. The entire cast is immensely talented, share amazing chemistry with one another, and are backed up by reoccurring guest-stars as other familiar characters from legend: Emilia Fox as Morgause, Santiago Cabrera as Lancelot, and John Hurt as the voice of the Great Dragon. With beautiful visuals and surprisingly good special effects, \\"Merlin\\" is suited for a family audience. There's nothing here that's too traumatizing for young viewers, though it does get darker as the series goes on.<br /><br />SOME SPOILERS FOLLOW. Nothing too drastic, more character-related than plot-related, and most of it pertaining to the first two episodes.<br /><br />*<br />*<br />*<br /><br />The third series picks up a year after the season two finale, during which the main cast has been diligently searching the kingdom for Morgana. Spirited away at the end of [[ASIN:B003ES5JII series two]] by her half-sister Morgause, only Merlin knows the true reason for her disappearance and his culpability in it (it's a bit of a long story, and obviously you'll want to watch the first two series before jumping headfirst into the third one). Arthur and Merlin are searching for her in enemy territory when a familiar figure stumbles out of the mist: Morgana! She is whisked back to Camelot, much to the joy of her foster-father Uther, but Merlin is not so sure she can be trusted. As an army marches upon Camelot at the behest of Morgause, it becomes apparent that there is a traitor within the walls of Camelot, a discovery that sets up the arc of the entire season.<br /><br />With thirteen episodes in all, season three of \\"Merlin\\" is a bit of a mixed bag - mostly good, but with a few missed opportunities that prevent it from being truly exceptional entertainment. But first, the good stuff. The series opener and finale (\\"The Tears of Uther Pendragon\\" and \\"The Coming of Arthur\\") are both two-parters, and are the best that the show has ever been. These four episodes are filmic in terms of the storytelling, visual effects and emotional punch on display. We get our clearest glimpse yet of the future that it to come, what with the reappearance of Excalibur and the Lady of the Lake, the introduction of the Round Table Knights, and that glorious final shot of...well, I'd better not give it away. It's wonderful, stirring stuff.<br /><br />Between our four young actors a great dynamic is at work. Arthur and Guinevere's love story begins to develop from puppy love into something more serious, and if you thought that the pairing was a little rushed in the second series, give it another chance. Here the writers allow the couple to flirt and laugh and enjoy each other's company, and as a result Bradley and Angel's chemistry feels much more natural. Meanwhile, Colin and Katie channel a very different kind of chemistry as former-friends Merlin and Morgana draw their line in the sand and find themselves on opposite sides. Their scenes together are nothing short of electrifying as Morgana strives to bring down the Pendragons and Merlin attempts to oppose her at every turn whilst protecting his own secret.<br /><br />Among the new characters that are introduced this season are the future Knights of the Round Table, providing heroics and eye-candy in equal measure. There are also some great episodes such as \\"The Crystal Cave\\" and \\"The Eye of the Phoenix\\" which do a great job of melding elements of Arthurian legend with the show's own vision.<br /><br />But...there's a downside. To my mind at least, there are two major problems at work in season three that prevent it from getting a five-star rating.<br /><br />The biggest disappointment of season three is what was meant to be its trump-card: Morgana. A prequel's success lies in the journey that characters take to predetermined destinations. We all KNOW where Merlin, Morgana, Arthur and Guinevere end up. What we're interested in is HOW they get to those places, and Morgana's fall from a spoilt but essentially good-hearted young woman to the evil enchantress of legend undoubtedly held the most potential for a dramatic character-arc.<br /><br />Morgana had been established as a brave, impulsive, passionate woman who was frightened by her growing powers and terrified of Uther's hatred of magic despite his love for her. We had seen for ourselves how dangerous she could be when crossed and how gifted she was with the art of manipulation. Hers was a personality that could quite easily be corrupted by fear and hatred, whilst still remaining sympathetic and defensible given her circumstances - after all, Morgana's main goal is to destroy a genocidal tyrant. For any writer, this set-up would be considered pure gold.<br /><br />And so it is a bitter disappointment to discover that the Morgana who returns to Camelot is not a dangerous and intelligent adversary with justifiable goals, divided loyalties and complex motivation, but rather nothing more than a petty caricature of evil. Her plans are illogical and convoluted, and thus fail without exception. She turns on Arthur and Guinevere without hesitation or remorse, taking sadistic pleasure in tormenting them for no apparent reason. There is a perpetual smirk on her face that nobody ever seems to notice. Despite the terrifying prospect of a traitor within the walls of Camelot, she poses no viable threat whatsoever and is continually thwarted by Merlin with almost laughable ease.<br /><br />Katie McGrath plays the part with relish, but for reasons best known to them, the writers took their most fascinating character and turned her into their most one-dimensional, void of any kind of sympathy, reason, or consistency. Not only has Morgana's past nuance been obliterated, but the events that could have explained this shift in personality took place entirely OFF SCREEN in the missing year between series two and three. It's a baffling creative decision, especially when one considers how fascinating her character-arc could have been.<br /><br />The other big disappointment of season three is the writer's steadfast refusal to move their plots forward. Much of the season is simply filler, and the stalling tactics are enough to wear the patience of any viewer. Every time there seems to be some sort of forward momentum in the story-arc, the writers veer off into irrelevant side-plots, skipping over what could have been fascinating material in favor of comedic farce, recycled plotlines, and one-shot guest stars. This has the added consequence of making the characters seem remarkably stupid, as they fail to follow up on leads or to ask questions that would have pushed the stories forward into new and interesting territory. For instance, it's ridiculous that neither Morgana nor Morgause think to question the fact that Merlin was able to escape the magical chains that they bind him with; or that Arthur and Uther wouldn't try to discover the identityof the traitor after the magical sabotage during the siege on Camelot.<br /><br />Why don't they do these things? Simply because if they did, the writers would be forced to move the story forward into places they (for whatever reason) don't want to go yet. Unfortunately it really achieves nothing except to make their characters seem dense, and waste a lot of good material.  Other times ideas are raised only to be ignored - there was a chance to explore Gwen's character further what with the introduction of her brother and her discovery of Morgana's magic, but nothing ever comes of these plot-points.<br /><br />The status-quo is finally shaken up at the end of the series, but by exorcising all the filler throughout the series, the events of \\"The Coming of Arthur\\" could have easily been stretched out over five or so episodes. We could have seen more of Morgana's reign, more of Gwen in the role of a spy, more of Uther's suffering, more of Arthur living rough in the woods, gathering his allies, and planning a counter-attack to win back his kingdom, more of Merlin and the future knights training and getting to know one another and working as a team to create La Resistance - you know, all the *interesting* stuff that inexplicably happens off-screen.<br /><br />Am I being too harsh? Maybe. \\"Merlin\\" has never pretended to be anything but fun and frivolous family entertainment, and in that it does the job. But so often it misses out on being something very brave and different, held back by the writers' (or producers') reluctance to give up the pointless standalone episodes in favor of a larger, more complex arc in which their characters can behave logically and develop naturally. With season four due to start very soon, I'm hoping that the show is finally ready to grow up and realize its potential.without exception. B005CM9DX6|5.0|We saw a hint of things to come with the brilliant \\"Watershed\\" a few years back.  While there were still a few measures of death metal roaring still present,  it was not hard to predict Mikeal Akerfeldt's breaking away from those grunting vocals and open the way for a huge boost in melody and meloncholy.  The album \\"Damnation\\" was also a clean vocals album but much more mild and frankly, for all its brilliance, a very mournful album fit for a cloudy drizzly day when the whole world seems to be in a funk.<br />  \\"Heritage\\" isn't a follow-up to \\"Damnation\\" so don't let those comparisons throw you off.  It's really quite simple.  Why grunt and growl when you have a voice like Akerfeldt's?  It is a waste of one of the most beautiful singing voices in popular music.  \\"Burden\\", from \\"Watershed\\" could and should have been a monster single, crashing into FM radio territory like the classic cut it is instead of being tucked away for Opeth fans. You can hear the thickness and the feel of the old analog sound, including somewhat muffled drums. B004LWZW4M|5.0|The movie was really great with a very plausible story and great acting by all.  It was casted and directed very well and echoes of the original POTA feature films but with a modern edge to it.  The movie ties in the original series where the apes actually DO take over where before, in the 70's versions, we do not actually see this.  And true to the original version, humans are affected by a virus although the original version has a different cause than the modern version.  I like this version better because it is far more convincing in the whole take over process.  What bothered me a bit is the overly animated movement of the chimps.  When an animal motion is animated in computer or hand drawn, it is once removed from life.  Then it is twice removed from life when it is transferred to film or some other media process.  So it is important for the animators to get it right without overdoing it but with enough \\"animated\\" behavior that we get it and are convinced by it.    I didn't quite feel that I was convinced and it wasn't because of the quality of the CG but rather it was the movements.  They were a bit unnatural in various parts of their actions.  The chimps didn't have the heft and weight to them and even some of their moves were overly exaggerated, particularly in Ceasar's behavior as a young chimp.  As a baby, there is very little movement in baby chimps, more clingy and subdued.  They have an instinct to coddle.  As Ceasar grew to be two and three years old, the movements were too rubbery and practically bouncing off the walls instead of looking like heavy, swift, and robust like he is supposed to be.  The apes throughout also moved way too quickly and lightly whereas apes in general have a more weightiness to them.  These animated apes seemed a bit too bouncy and almost too wiggly at times.  This was especially true of the orangutangs.  Orangutangs generally move very slowly and these guys were like spider monkies when they swung around.   Before you start beating me up for my comments, (I teach animal motion and biomechanics in an animal drawing and anatomy class for a university and many of my students are 2-D artists and 3-D animators, just so you know.  I've studied animal motion and behavior for years - and even train animals.  My experience may be more limited than some but enough to know that some of the action was a bit more exaggerated and it did'nt have to be!  That being said, it was a really great film and I eventually lost my critiqing part of my brain after a while and settled in to the movie.  The story was written extremely well.  It was fresh while still keeping step with the 70's original tone.  It was a convincing story, acted well, and had humanity elements in it, fitting for our concerns of our modern times.  The full gamut of human emotions played through this movie, ribboning its way through wave after wave.  Love and compassion pendulated between rage and extreme frustration, we see the world through Ceasar's eyes and his scientist human.  In the end there is the final the taste of sweet revenge and freedom.  The action and plots played out at a great pace also and there were no segways or layers that were not relevant. The movie is pretty fast paced and exciting all the way!  Amazingly done well and absolutely fantastic was the facial expressions that came across so convincingly.  The apes had amazingly realistic expressions (and they had to because they lacked story telling dialog) and their emotions were so clearly read.  The acting with the eyes and body language rang so clear that even a human could read the emotions from their posture and gaze.  Real apes do display various emotional expressions without contorting their faces but this movie went a step further.  Ceasar was a far more sophisticated creature than his brothers so his face revealed a higher intellectual process and you can really see the wheels turning in his eyes!  What a fantastic job!   The whole movie was an amazing production for everyone involved.  Especially the actors and digital animators - they should really be commended and get a award for this.  Awesome job!  There were more layers in this movie than the straight line of the original ape sagas.  The multi-layered story worked well and everything wrapped into other layers, coming together at the end.  It is a great movie in spite of my critical comments, and I'm buying my copy soon!
