0060558121|5.0|Neil Gaiman has become one of those rare breed of writers for whom many people will buy his books as soon as they see his name on the cover. And this is with good reason. He can write superbly well. His characterisations are interesting, his narrative is entertaining and his dialogue is frequently downright hilarious. This book is as good an example of that as almost any (I preferred the Anansi Boys though, which I read before this. There are shared themes between these two books).<br /><br />In American Gods, we are introduced to Shadow - a convict who is released early on compassionate grounds following the death of his wife. Almost immediately he is met by a man who calls himself Wednesday - and is clearly much more than just an ordinary man. Wednesday wants Shadow's help, but he is not specific as to what he wants - only mentioning that it is very dangerous. Indeed, early in the book there are all kinds of things that we are not told. Why exactly was Shadow in prison? Who is Wednesday (actually I guessed that one straight away), what does he want? What happened to Shadow's wife? And much more.<br /><br />The answers to these questions mostly do not come early. But they do come. There is a road trip across America that put me strongly in mind of Stephen King's \\"The Talisman\\". The protagonists are quite different, but I was reminded of the other book because of the natural/supernatural duality and the journey across America, experiencing different places - some more special than others. The whole story, it seemed to me, could as easily have been penned by Stephen King.<br /><br />There are two reasons I do not think, in good consience, that I can give this five stars (even though Neil Gaiman fans will therefore start voting down my review! But please don't without leaving me a comment as to why!) These are:<br /><br />1) This was a very earthy novel. There is plenty of sex - especially oral sex - mentioned. Many people will like that in a book, but I did not think that for the most part it added anything to the story. The language likewise was earthier than was strictly necessary, and moreso than in the Anansi Boys, which I preferred to this book. (I also thought the Anansi Boys was funnier).<br /><br />2) This book was almost ponderously long. Ultimately there were two plot themes, but the middle of the book spent so long setting them up that the resolution was very brief. One plot was resolved in a couple of pages and was something of a non event. The other was resolved in a longer epilogue. But did the book need to be 670 pages long? Neil Gaiman admits the book is long but excuses it by saying that America is a big country, which is true. The narrative is wonderfully descriptive of America - but whether that was all necessary for this story is an open question.<br /><br />So in summary - a very good book. I can recommend it, although I preferred the Anansi Boys, and that would be the book of Gaiman's I handed adult readers first. (The Graveyard Book would be my pick for young adults or those who enjoy young adult books).thing to the story. 0307472841|1.0|I've become a fan of Henning Mankell's Wallender stories, mostly through the British TV series. The gritty depiction of Swedish culture and country is fascinating, and the plots are laid out well enough to sustain my interest. This story is not part of the Wallender series, however.   This book begins with a gruesome mass murder in a remote village, and the author captures the isolation and atmosphere of the place. Even though the incident is over the top, it's an interesting premise from which to begin.   However, the author then steps outside Sweden and begins to go on at length about international politics, economics, and history. A major portion of the book is set in China, and it's quickly clear that his knowledge of that country goes no deeper than a few newspaper headlines. He has his characters expound in lengthy, boring monologues on the virtues of Communism, the wisdom of Chairman Mao and the political situation inside China. He would have the reader believe not only that Chinese peasants are ready to revolt, but that the Chinese government has developed a plan to solve the problem by relocating them to Africa. Actually, there are almost a billion peasants in China- sending a few million of them to Africa wouldn't even begin to address the issue, even if it were real. The whole structure is not only not credible- it's boring. Even the ending is bad- abrupt and unsatisfying.  I look forward to Mr. Mankell returning to the things he does well. I certainly won't read any more of his stories which take place outside Sweden.

This will only work well in rooms that are not very bright behind the monitor, and if your monitor is in close proximity to the wall. B002ZG98C8|4.0|&#34;Machete&#34; is one of the first entries of Hollywood's &#34;supergroup cinema&#34; trend, mixing lead performers from different rungs of the cinematic ladder in a bombastic vehicle that would've been unthinkable to produce in preceding decades. It also continues the fad of retrofitting modern action movies with exploitation twists in emulation of grandhouse features. In both arenas, &#34;Machete&#34; succeeds remarkably, making it one of the more enjoyable features of 2010.<br /><br />The story: Three years after the murder of his family at the hands of a ruthless drug lord (Steven Seagal, [[ASIN:B00003CXW4 Exit Wounds]]), ex-Federale &#34;Machete&#34; Cortez (Danny Trejo, [[ASIN:B0000WN140 Once Upon a Time in Mexico]]) becomes a wild card in a shady conflict involving the attempted assassination of a Texas Senator (Robert De Niro, [[ASIN:0790729725 GoodFellas]]), an underground immigrant aide network run by a tough beauty (Michelle Rodriguez, [[ASIN:B002VPE1AW Avatar]]), and a plot to exploit both to dark ends.<br /><br />The cast is the first thing that catches your eye, and for the most part it's remarkably well-chosen. Danny Trejo - the perpetually underrated tough-guy - plays his first mainstream starring role to the hilt. De Niro brings a sense of legitimacy to the picture and proves that he doesn't mind not being the center of attention. Rodriguez is cool throughout but positively explodes during the finale. Seagal's casting is the most controversial but also the cleverest: eight years of DTV features have done nothing to lessen his intensity, and he plays a bad guy with deliciously despicable hamminess. Jeff Fahey as the two-faced co-villain, Cheech Marin as the shotgun-toting padre, and Don Johnson as the twisted nationalist militia leader are a hoot to watch, and even Lindsay Lohan gets her share of awesome moments. It's so much fun to see all these performer together. The only performer who doesn't do too well is Jessica Alba ([[ASIN:B00005JNTX Sin City]]) as the conflicted policewoman: her part's written well enough, but she doesn't seem into it and gives a spotty performance.<br /><br />The film's grindhouse style is mostly consistent, but it lapses whenever the filmmakers realize they have to focus more on the story or purposely let on that they're not taking themselves seriously. Stylistic aging of the video quality is attempted early on but quickly dropped. The violence is bloody but rarely outright gruesome: even when Machete slices open a man's stomach and pulls the length of his intestines out (he uses 'em as a rope), it's sold as incredibly fake and shouldn't bother anyone besides the extra squeamish. The actual action content is pretty cool: gunfights, explosions, knife fights, and many impromptu beheadings by Machete meld together to form a veritable action opus. Surprisingly, Steven Seagal's character gets in on very little of this: he's most active late in the movie, when he engages Machete in a swordfight that's neat but not nearly as furious as the other ones he's had in his career. Spectacle is occasionally played over substance here, but I can't fault these scenes: any gunfight that convinces me that Lindsay Lohan is hardcore automatically deserves credit.<br /><br />The film's political overtones regarding the exploitation of illegal labor in the U.S. are, in my opinion, justified but could easily limit its appeal among viewers with different opinions. (Anyone who frequented this one's forum on IMDb years ago will remember all the Henny-Pennies who cried that it was going to initiate a race war.) Nevertheless, I don't consider this a serious political movie - just a fun, reckless action flick with a bit of style. Even if this one doesn't become a cult classic, director Robert Rodriguez ought to be proud of his work.icted policewoman: her part's written well enough, but she doesn't seem into it and gives a spotty performance. 0670022411|2.0|'A Discovery of Witches' was quite a surprise, and not in a good way. All the glowing reviews really made me believe that I was about to have an unforgettable experience, after all, I enjoyed Twilight with its corny and incredibly romantic plot, and many mentioned that this was the adult version of Edward and Bella's story. Unfortunately, even though the novel had a lot of promise, the execution was greatly lacking. Both the characters and plot had many inconsistencies and the story did not flow as smoothly as it should have. Here's why.   1. Characterization. The first few chapters were well crafted. Diana's character was so refreshing, and I absolutely loved her reserved and meticulous manner along with the well-constructed reasoning for why she chose the path of a scholar. Matthew's first introduction depicted him as a self-serving, contemptuous elitist with a very strict ethical code for a vampire.   So where did those personae disappear? A third of the way into the book, Diana became a whiny, thoughtless and unreasonably dense teenager instead of a thirty-something adult woman. She let a vampire she barely knew, whose kind she was taught to hate from childhood, literally bully her into going to his estate in France to avoid a perceived danger. There she was alternatively treated with contempt, pity and intimidation by various Clairmont Clan members, while she thoughtlessly brushed it off and worried about her appearance. Also, the constant tiredness and overeating made no sense for a lifelong athlete. Later on when she went back to New York, her treatment of her aunts who raised her over Mathew, whom she knew for a couple of weeks, was ridiculous. She was highhanded with Sarah and Em and became a worshiping mouse with occasional outbursts of spirit in front of Mathew. Inconsistent to say the least.   Matthew's character went through an even bigger transformation, going from Alpha Male to a love-struck puppy with temper issues. His behavior, including but not limited to intimacy issues, senseless protectiveness, inability to make logical and tactical decisions, fear of killing Diana, trying to alternatively push her away and keep her close, made no sense. The change in his personality from what was originally introduced was so drastic that I felt like I was reading about someone with serious identify crisis. I also could not understand why logical Diana found it so endearing, especially her acceptance of his vigilante streak. The breaking point for me was during Diana's rescue, when both his mother and brother kept on reeling him back from useless attempts of finding and saving her. Was I really reading about a 1500 years old vampire who was a personal friend of every notable person throughout history? On top of that, the abstinence was just ridiculous. Chapter after chapter of narratives about their platonic relationship added nothing to the story, although it was amusing to see Diana throwing herself at him to be rebuffed at every turn.  Also, constant encounters/accounts of his past love interests once again added nothing to the story or gave any useful insight about his character. They felt more like filler material to make the book longer than plot devices to further the reader's enjoyment.  2. Story. The premise for the book was well constructed.  Vampires, witches, daemons and humans were introduced as separate species, and all subject to Darwin's theory of evolution. While the history of human transformation was well documented by scientists over the years, the other groups lacked such understanding, and gradual population decline forced some to take action in discovering the truth. Not to give more of the plot away, this excellent foundation was met with poor execution. I think my main complaint was lack of foreshadowing. We kept on being fed new information throughout the book and the author left nothing for us to discover on our own. It was like an instruction manual on Diana's life, hence it didn't draw me intothe story. It was also the main reason why Twilight was successful for me and `A Discovery of Witches' was a failure.  I loved that part in Twilight where the whole story gradually builds to the culmination originally foreshadowed at the beginning of the book. When I was reading it, I tried to imagine how Bella's original words would come to life, and it made it all the powerful when I finally read it.    Overall, 'A Discovery of Witches' lacked subtlety in execution. The protagonists were always a step behind and clueless, while antagonist's actions were sporadic and needlessly sadistic. Bad guys would show up occasionally, wreak havoc and then disappear for a duration, which allowed team Matthew and Diana to regroup and work on her magic. Why didn't they ever come in force? They knew from pretty much the start that they weren't going to get cooperation from Diana and were frequently depicted as being ruthless, powerful and cunning, so why not strike against them when their defenses were at their weakest? These types of blunders could have been forgiven in a paperback harlequin romance, but I found them intolerable in a 600 page action packed paranormal mystery series.  I struggled to the last page and will not be purchasing the sequel. I gave this book 2 stars only because the plot had so much promise.

B00004R7X5|4.0|This disc features three of Russia's most familiar composition: Rimsky-Korsakov's Scheherazade and Tchaikovsky's 1812 Overture and Capriccio Italien.  They are all so popular that almost everybody will recognize their famous tunes.  So, of course, it should come as a breath of fresh air when musicians as great as Karajan and the Berliners try to tackle these masterpieces.  Did they succeed in giving us something special?<br /><br />Well, they certainly did in the Scheherazade.  This is a work overflowing with a wealth of melodies combined with gripping drama.  Karajan and the Berliners seem right at home in such a world.  Karajan is gloriously intense, seeing all of the many mood changes, delivering them with finesse, while preventing things from getting out of hand (one of his greatest gifts as a conductor).  Everyone wants to be kept awake during this work, and Karajan does the job.  Clarity reigns even in the most climatic of moments, the buildup to the sinking of the ship in particular.  Karajan has a wonderful eye for detail and even though this disc is from the 60's, you can pick up on wonderful contrasts that are present throughout.  And I shouldn't forget to mention the win of the performance--the 3rd movement.  Here the Berlin strings take us out of this world with the power of their beauty and elegance.  I didn't know such a thing was possible in this work, but it appears that it is.  Despite this recording's age, it is just as indispensible now as it was when it first appeared on LP.<br /><br />After hearing the Scheherazade, the Tchaikovsky sounds unsatisfying in comparison.  Karajan is still intense, to be sure, but I don't hear him pulling out as many thrilling moments.  Sometimes the music can border on sounding dull, and that is not what I want in my Tchaikovsky.  It's strange, because Karajan has worked wonders with Tchaikovsky elsewhere.  Perhaps he, like me, considers these works, particularly the 1812, to be overhyped and doesn't have much interest in the music.  The average listener won't notice these setbacks, because things are still interesting to a degree, but experienced listeners will probably complain.  In order for these works to really make an impression, solemnity will be out the window.  But that is exactly what Karajan tries to incorporate, which keeps things from really coming to life.  It wouldn't have hurt to throw more fun into the picture.  At least that's how I feel.<br /><br />To summarize, the Scheherazade is given a stunning performance that demands a listen, while the Tchaikovsky asks for more.  But given the quality of the former work, you've got to own this disc.  The recording quality is very good considering that it's from 1967.  Buy this CD as if though you were only getting the Scheherazade, and there's no way you'll be disappointed.The average listener won't notice these setbacks, because things are still interesting to a degree, but experienced listeners will probably complain. B0000YB7Z8|5.0|Jethro Tull's 1978 live album Bursting Out is one of those rare and fantastic live albums where the sound and energy are so fantastic that many the tracks eclipse their studio counterparts, where the guitars are louder and heavier and the drums are harder and more forceful.<br /><br />Bursting Out finds the band on tour supporting their Heavy Horses album and as such contains an absolute wealth of material that fans of the band's work from the 1970s will love, representing most of their albums up to that point with at least one track and delivering a lot of the classics side by side.<br /><br />Highlights include an astounding twelve minute rendition of `Thick As A Brick,' as well as the louder and heavier versions of `Sweet Dream,' `No Lullaby,' and `A New Day Yesterday,' however the whole record is absolutely essential listening.<br /><br />In addition to the harder rock moments, like `Cross Eyed Mary,' and `Hunting Girl,' there are acoustic favourites like `Jack In The Green,' `One Brown Mouse,' and `Skating Away On The Thin Ice Of The New Day,' that work brilliantly along side the heavier tracks and also both a big Flute solo and Drum solo to display the member's immense talents... not that everyone wasn't doing that anyway adding great flourishes of exemplary musicianship throughout the proceedings.<br /><br />The performance is absolutely phenomenal with big exciting drum fills, creative guitar solos and Ian's usual astounding Flute and Vocal work on a particularly high standard here.<br /><br />Overall, Bursting Out is a fantastic live record, a great representation of the band's 1970s progressive period and would serve as a fine introduction to the band indeed for potential fans. It is one of the best Jethro Tull albums period. If you like Jethro Tull at all then get yourself a copy.

I mean, it really  grates under my skin.  She is actually offended that people find her sexually attractive.  WHATEVER.  I've been to high school.  That isnt a bad thing.  I would understand if she had had lifelong sexual abuse, and the remarks continued to objectify her. But even she admitted that her parents loved her.  They were just distracted.  The book just didnt ring true.  This girl had no reason whatsoever, no motive in legal speak.  I find it rather demeaning that people find this so indicative of a real suicidal person.  I've been in much darker places that this child.  With God's help, I made it out.  I dont know how people make it, when they refuse to rely on God.  Maybe it is the fact that I'm far removed from being a teenager, and far removed from that high drama, everything is life or death mentality.  Her character was so weak and whiny.  I kept waiting for more, for real reasons.  They just didnt come.  And when she hid in the closet at the party while her classmate was being raped, it was  just sickening.  But she could have told the cops, could have got counseling.  I feel far more sympathy for poor Clay.
B002BEXG86|5.0|How many bands can crank out not one but TWO career bests within a single calendar year? The original Alice Cooper group did just that back in 1971 when their second album with producer Bob Ezrin was released that November.<br /><br />Just as raw as \\"Love It To Death\\", \\"Killer\\" possesses some new touches that they would expand on the following year with \\"School's Out\\".<br /><br />\\"Under My Wheels\\" is as arresting an album opener as you can get. It still amazes me this song never became a driving classic like \\"Born To Be Wild\\". Can the car metaphors be any more obvious?<br /><br />\\"Be My Lover\\" is the band's autobiographical laid-back rocker (\\"She asked me why the singer's name was Alice. I said listen baby, you really wouldn't understand\\").<br /><br />The big showpiece of the album is the epic \\"Halo of Flies\\", which is really three songs in one, including a beautiful overture halfway through. The galloping drums of Neal Smith during Act I is probably my favorite part.<br /><br />Alice's sharp vocals make for a great contrast against the gorgeous strings on \\"Desperado\\".<br /><br />The distorted, kicking \\"You Drive Me Nervous\\" is Alice at his head-banging best.<br /><br />The album's low point would have to be the somewhat clumsy and lazily titled \\"Yeah, Yeah, Yeah\\", which is the only song on \\"Killer\\"--or \\"Love It To Death\\" for that matter--that I would even remotely consider \\"filler\\". But even it is much better than half of the material on the \\"School's Out\\" album.<br /><br />Next to \\"Halo of Flies\\", the infamous, atmospheric \\"Dead Babies\\" which opens with Dennis Dunaway's ominous bassline and features plenty of gritty guitar work, is probably the album's next best cut.<br /><br />The album ends on a high note with Alice channelling Jim Morrison on the title track, which like \\"Halo of Flies\\" is like a three-part opera which ultimately ends up with Alice being led to his death in the gallows before the supremely-jarring finale.<br /><br />\\"Killer\\" is just as great an album as \\"Love It To Death\\"--in some ways even better. If you want to experience the original ACG at their best, it's these two albums you want.album as \\"Love It To Death\\"--in some ways even better. 1451626657|5.0|It's sobering to realise that it's 50 years since Joseph Heller's biting satire `Catch 22' was first published. Through the 1960s and 70s, its famous title was slowly adopted into everyday idiomatic usage around the world and is now quoted by people who have never read the book and may not even know the origin of `Catch 22' to mean any `double-bind' situation designed to get you no matter which way you try to brook the system.<br /><br />Heller's novel is themed on the way an unfeeling bureaucracy conspires to marginalize the individual. It covers the same ground as much of Franz Kafka's work (notably `The Trial') and has an Orwellian core, but `Catch 22' is much funnier than almost any other work of this loose genre and is a joy to read.<br /><br />Earnest comedy combines with episodic chopped-up storytelling: on re-reading the book after several decades, it becomes obvious that Heller does in fact employ a tight and disciplined - though unorthodox - structure, where common themes are continually re-stated and reinforced with humor and for example the same scenes are described from the perspectives of different characters as the novel unfolds. Clever observation of the absurdity of bureaucratic thinking through a host of sharply-drawn and fleshed-out characters display human behavioural psychology in a way few other novels of the modern age manage to do. You put people in a situation like this, and this is what can happen: it all has its own peculiar logic where the insane can be comfortably in charge and the irrational appears normal. If you are prepared to exploit the system you can get away with almost anything, whereas if you follow the rules and demonstrate `patriotism' and `honor' - well, you're basically screwed.<br /><br />The characters who populate Yossarian's world are hard to forget: General Dreedle, the Chaplain, ex-P.F.C Wintergreen, Major Major Major, (who had a rank created just for him, to exploit his unique name); the incomparable Milo Minderbender and his grandiose money-making syndicate extending even to trading with the enemy and contracting to fly missions for them; the comparatively sane and rational Doc Daneeka, who explains Catch 22 to Yossarian; Nately, Cathcart and the rest.<br /><br />You could describe `Catch 22' as a comic-tragedy, but above all it's instructive and entertaining. If you've never read this 20th century classic and real, solid books with paper pages are your thing, you now have the chance to get yourself a new edition of the long-out-of-print paperback- the `50th Anniversary Edition'. Go on, treat yourself.king syndicate extending even to trading with the enemy and contracting to fly missions for them; the comparatively sane and rational Doc Daneeka, who explains Catch 22 to Yossarian; Nately, Cathcart and the rest. B003ZSJ212|1.0|Well, the original Star Wars movies that so many people experienced as they first appeared in cinemas are apparently no longer of value or relevant to anyone anymore and are not included in this set.  The fact that a quality print of the original Star Wars is still not available today is unbelievable and a real loss to fans of the original trilogy, and indeed to all those with a genuine interest in the preservation of important works and maintaining their integrity within film history.   I am sure this release will adequately meet expectations for those that enjoy the revised canon, and I genuinely hope it is all that you wish for, but also feel that many people are greatly missing out by not experiencing the original theatrical versions, which actually had a more natural looking colour pallet, better contrast with no detail loss in shadows, and pioneering special effects and model work still intact. I have great memories of watching Star Wars when first shown in cinemas all those years ago, and the real sense of adventure it evoked in the imagination, and although obviously a little sad that both new and older fans will not be able enjoy the original trilogy at a respectable quality, I believe this release really does mark what is a great missed opportunity. 0060763280|3.0|On a whim, I picked up this book for cheap in a used bookstore and don't regret it. The advice given by T. Harv Eker is conducive to potentially earning more money and even building character. Some of Eker's tips are well known but at times hard to put into practice: positive inner thoughts projecting out into the world; fresh ways of thinking to enhance happiness and success; dropping all unsupportive past money habits to increase earnings. Eker inserts the usual cheerleading platitudes such as committing yourself to wealth, retaining only empowering thoughts, thinking large and setting specific goals. These are all admirable objectives, but what I admired most about \\"Millionaire Mind\\" were Eker's points about saving money (with multiple accounts), investing (making sure to educate yourself, diversify and remain in it for the long haul), keeping track of your overall net worth and simplifying your life to keep more money. It sometimes gets forgotten that millionaires don't have to be flashy people; a modest home and lifestyle saves a lot of money in the grand scheme, which can be used for other things (entertainment, necessities, saving or investing).<br /><br />The author also conveys that earning a fortune takes creativity, honesty and lots of hard work. He recommends a part- or full-time venture on your own so that you can eventually be in business for yourself, and Eker is big on selling, which he says people can earn a fortune from. Eventually, he says, if you work hard enough and get involved with the right things, your money will work for you and you can sit back and enjoy. This is easier said than done, of course, but it can be accomplished by thinking outside the box and courageously moving forward.<br /><br />Based on his book and website, Harv Eker comes across as a genuine guy with a streamlined message, someone who thankfully doesn't offer guidance about everything under the sun. His language in the book is cheerful and straightforward, maybe even a bit too direct for some people's taste. He unabashedly promotes his seminars, but that's par for the course since he recommends heavily marketing what you have to offer. His advice doesn't reinvent the wheel, and much of this stuff may already be well known to many. But for me, a lot of Eker's information was reinforcing and, at times, inspirational.some people's taste. 0596805527|1.0|The book starts out explaining data types, objects, assignments, conversions, comparison, scoping etc.., Essentially, the fundamental semantics at the very lowest level or the highest level of resolution of the language. But why the painful level of detail? It is good to know these things but the detail is unnecessary. This book reminds me of a \\"Programming Languages\\" course I once took. These details are good to be referred to while laying out how to avoid the caveats with some good programming practices. The theme is \\"Everything about  JavaScript Semantics that could every be known\\" instead of \\"Here is how to write JavaScript code and here is how  you code it to avoid these potential ambiguities\\". I am fortunate to have a CS degree and I understand the issues and can develop good programming practices on-the-fly as I read. What I want to know in this order is.  1. Here is a facility of the language.  2. Here is what it means without all the caveats.  3. Here is how you write it.  4. Be careful about this... and that....( major caveats here )  5. More int the Appendix for debugging.  Instead,  It is a very dry read and no doubt with the number of peculiarities in JavaScript regarding these topics, it becomes clear that:  1. You will probably not remember all of these rules right away.  2. JavaScript being not strongly typed will allow even the best programmer to create small but potentially painful and hard to detect bugs.  3. Convince the programmer to use mnemonics to distinguish the object class/type but never states this explicitly. Especially when it comes to objects v. variables due to the immutability of variables and the peculiar behaviors associated with treating variables like objects.  4. The data type conversion rules are very similar to other languages but then there are peculiarities that may cause #3 above type bugs to painfully appear.  5. Explicit data type conversion functions can be just as confusing.  6. But he never really lays down any rules of thumb to avoid ambiguity and future bugs.   7. Even the brightest people do not hold all of these rules in their head.   8. The programmer is left to defining a set of best practices to avoid ambiguity of conversion, comparison, assignment and evaluation. Truly a lacking aspect of this book. The easy way out of all of the plethora of possible bugs are good programming practices. The author never brings this up.  9. When discussing the scope of a variable  local v. global ), the author is exceptionally ambiguous in his descriptions of JavaScript scoping. He does mention one good practice regarding scoping. However, there is a very simple solution to this. He also includes code like this:  function f  {   var scope;   // Local variable is declared at the top of the function  console.log(scope);   // It exists here, but still has \\"undefined\\" value   scope = \\"local\\";   // Now we initialize it and give it a value   console.log(scope);   // And here it has the value we expect   }   I ask only one question here. Why doesn't the author include the expected value in the comment. This kind of repetition sends the concept home. There are loose ends everywhere in his book where he makes statements like, \\" it is not what we expect \\" without any further explanation. I am lucky that I am an experienced programmer and I can look back over the code to verify what my understanding. But why should I? It would be nice to read continuously through this book without hoping on promises that it will be covered in a later chapter or having to look back over the code again.  I am beginning to like this book less and less as I read it. Come on .... , just lay out the problem in a very simple way and show how to code around it. This book is obsessed with expressing the knowledge of caveats without a basic set of coding standards to avoid them. It would be much better if we could learn the JavaScript and learn good coding without obsessing on the caveats. I am on Chapter 4 so far and there has been no discussion on programming. We have discussed loops and functions in regard to scoping but not how to write them.   I am down to one star on the first day of reading. I should not have to chop their way through the Amazon Jungle twice. Use the same trail over again. I feel like I have paid for a guide who insists on chopping a new trail through the jungle instead of using one over again. Give me a break. All of these rules without any mnemonics or rules of thumb. I can come up with them myself  but I feel like I am reading a dictionary.   So far, other than laying out the bare facts the book is not at all inspirational.   Continued later ....

0553579908|5.0|Anyone who says that Martin is not in the same league as Tolkien is missing something. A Song of Ice and Fire is fantasy of a high, fine order, fantasy in the tradition of Harry Potter.<br /><br />What I mean by that is that GRRM makes his fantasy highly accessible. In the same way Rowling used humour and whimsy to introduce us to his (Potter's) world, Martin uses language and detail to pull us headlong into his. The horrors of war are placed alongside the mystery and wonder of youth, the magic of discovery, and the real progression of time slipping away. Martin's heroes are not all powerful lords of the world, they are not high-level sorcerors and knights that can wade through enemies without a scratch. Many of his heroes are not even heroes at all. They reside in that grey space that defines real life - they are simply people driven by their motivations. Love. Lust. Greed. Piety. Ambition. We see all of this from the subjective point of view of the people experiencing it.<br /><br />Martin also shows himself to have a deep understanding of how we communicate - and miscommunicate. Information is scattered, disparate. We see (through his character's eyes) how confusing and muddled the world may have been in the days of knights and kings, when information traveled much more slowly and disinformation was almost the law of the land.<br /><br />The interconnectedness of Martin's world lends a realism that even Jordan somehow failed to capture. In this book even more so than A Game of Thrones we see the real costs of a war, its real effects on people. The subjective nature of the story makes even the high fantasy elements (magic, dragons, and alchemy for starters) seem realistic. This is magic of the Stephen King variety - powerful, real, usable, but not necessarily to be trusted, and never fully understood.<br /><br />If you haven't read A Game of Thrones, you must do so before reading A Clash of Kings. It's essential reading to understand the character dynamics in thisbook. All in all though, the entire series is a \\"must read\\" for any true fan of fantasy or simple good fiction.

0307887898|4.0|Author Eric Ries a computer programmer by trade and his greatest business success has been a company called IMVU, an instant messaging service that allows people to communicate using avatars. He uses his experiences as the basis for the main lessons in The Lean Startup, as well as other entrepreneurial stories from the technology industry.  The main point of this book is that business start-ups tend to be much higher-risk endeavors than they need to be. Entrepreneurs in every setting make the same mistakes. They build elaborate products before testing them with consumers. They make decisions based on the wrong information, and they stick with bad ideas long after they should.   According to Ries, a start-up is a \\"human institution designed to create a new product or service under conditions of extreme uncertainty\\". The key words here are \\"human\\" and \\"uncertainty\\".  A successful start-up does not just rely on a brilliant idea, but also requires managing people through all the challenges of innovation and growth, and through times when the idea will fail and when people will fight over what to do next.  Ries suggests that entrepreneurs create a \\"minimal viable product\\" as soon as they can and start testing it with consumers. It will be worth a thousand hours of strategy planning and internal analysis. Then they should keep testing every part of their business models using \\"split testing\\" - a technique taken from direct advertising, whereby you test slightly different versions of the same product with different groups of customers to see which works best.  I really enjoyed the author's to-the-point writing style.  This book is full of useful ways to rethink start-ups for any business.  If you are thinking of starting a new business, or if you are thinking of changing the direction of your business, this will give you plenty of good ideas and probably save you time and money in the process.

0156027321|5.0|Life of Pi took a couple of tries to get me interested.  Pi has some personal theories and ideals about religion, instinct, and force of character he wants to express in addition to the story line.  The resulting complexity ultimately gives this book the depth that makes it worth reading.  In places the book gives a sense of excessive description, which perhaps controls the pace of the story but fails to enrich Pi's character.  As a weary bedtime reader, this led me to put the book down with no urgency to return.  When I finally picked it up at dinner one night and read it at speed, the story held together well.  I could easily ignore the thin parts of the plot and focus on the overwhelming presence of the tiger, for whatever it symbolizes.  While the plot thinned at times, the emotional power and character development remained credible and coherent.  Pi's ideas about religion, instinct and force of character have enough depth to challenge the reader to improve upon them, and the symbolism is blurry enough to support multiple angles of argument. B004LWZVZC|3.0|\\"The Conspirator\\" is NOT an entertainment. I'm hardly the first to say so; most of the 80-some reviews already posted have taken note of the film's political agenda. Though the script ostensibly depicts the military trial, conviction, and execution of Mary Surratt -- in whose boarding house in Washington DC the assassination of Abraham Lincoln and the attempted assassination of William Seward were plotted -- a viewer of this film would need to be utterly unaware of current events not to grasp that the film is implicitly a denunciation of the actions of the George W Bush administration during the \\"War on Terror\\". I have no doubt that director Robert Redford and script writer James Solomon intended a connection to be understood between the suspension of habeas corpus in 1863, during the Civil War, and the assaults on civil liberties of the Bush/Cheney neo-conservatives in the name of national security. Yes, there are points of relevant comparison, but unfortunately there are overlapping agendas. I'd love to avoid ranking this preachment-disguised-as-a-film with stars, but I can't. So, it gets one star for muddle or five stars for good intentions, but understand, please, that the ranking is arbitary and could go either way. Therefore, my three stars ... a 'mugwump' solution if ever!<br /><br />First I have to be honest about my own agendas. I'm opposed to capital punishment for any crime under any circumstances, although my opposition is totally rational rather than emotional. There are acts so unspeakably hideous that I would relish shredding the perpetrators with a cheese-grater, but I don't believe judicial murder serves the good of society. The hanging of Mary Surratt, the climax of this film, is patently intended to draw my/your sympathy to a woman who may have been unfairly tried and unjustly punished. Had I been one of the panel of judges in 1865, I would not have voted for her execution, nor for the execution of the other three convicted plotters. I would have stuck with my conscience. But the sympathy for Mary Surratt is merely a ploy in this film, a distractor, a red herring if you will. In fact, the evidence against Mary Surratt is far more than circumstantial. She was guilty, in that she certainly knew what was being plotted. Even Redford and Solomon can't help allowing for her guilt. So the \\"agenda\\" here isn't opposition to capital punishment or supposition that Mary Surratt was innocent.<br /><br />That brings us back to Bush/Cheney. And that puts me on the spot, because I fiercely agree with the implications of the film. Bush/Cheney violated the provisions of the Constitution and the Bill of Rights. The prison camp at Guantanamo, the use of torture, the kidnappings -- all villainies and violations of the best American ideals! Take a look at the other one/two star reviews of The Conspirator, and you'll find that supporters of the Bush/Cheney doctrines denounce the film's agenda. And here I am, supporting that agenda yet disapproving of the film! What's up?<br /><br />John Surratt Jr., Mary's son, fled from Washington before the assassination which he had certainly helped to plot. In the film, his mother pleads with him not to go, not to do anything reckless, and he responds that \\"the War isn't over yet\\". Oh boy, was he right! The War was won on battlefields but it wasn't over in the \\"hearts and minds\\" of Americans northern or southern. It still isn't over; a good pollster could easily show a strong correlation between opinions about the Civil War and the ideological divisions between Liberals and Conservatives (both mislabeled) in the USA today.<br /><br />I suspect that one's reaction to this film will be determined by one's understandings of the Civil War and Reconstruction. Viewers with Southern, States' rights sympathies would thus be expected to acclaim the film as a sound denunciation of 'Big Government' tyranny. The depiction of Secretary of War Edwin Stanton as an unscrupulous tyrant would suit their prejudices perfectly; he was, after all, a \\"radical reconstructionist\\". However, such viewers soon run into a dilemma. Most of them are quite ready to side with Bush/Cheney over the so-called Patriot Act and the use of torture. Which agenda comes first?<br /><br />The film muddles diverse agendas too much for my taste. The Civil War was NOT a conflict over States' Rights; that was always a rhetorical smokescreen for other agendas. The Civil War was about slavery,and it's problematical for me that slavery is not even given an allusion in the film. One can argue that the War was not fought by the North to free the slaves, but the other side of the issue is plain as sin. From the perspective of the South, it was a war fought to preserve and extend slavery. The assassination of Abraham Lincoln was the dramatic first blow in the perpetuation of the Civil War  -- Johnny Surratt got that right -- which reversed the Northern victory in battle. I'm not being original; it's been said since the 1870s that \\"the North won the War but lost the Peace\\". The violence that thwarted Reconstruction, the ugly 'Redemption' and disenfranchisement of the slaves and their descendants, the Jim Crow apartheid and racial cleansing violence of the South for the next hundred years, and the still-too-evident racism of so many in America ... Hey, it's painfully clear that Edwin Stanton, even in this slanderous portrayal, had a point. Not only the assassins and their accessories, like Mary Surratt, were guilty. The entire Confederacy, military and civilian, got unmerited mercy and used it chiefly for revenge.<br /><br />Who can sincerely believe that the virulent attacks on the character of Barack Obama are not at least significantly an expression of racism? These days, 150 years afterwards, Civil War Consciousness is far stronger among Southerners (those of Southern heritage, not those who happen now to live in the South) than among non-Southerners (I use that negative designation to include the many who have no particularly image of themselves as Northerners). \\"The South Will Rise Again!\\" The Myth of the Lost Cause is still taught in schools and homes. Take a look at the mendacious twaddle in \\"The Politically Incorrect Guide to the South (and Why It Will Rise Again)\\" by Clint Johnson or the \\"PIG to American History\\" by Thomas E. Woods. I'd wager that both Johnson and Woods would have ferociously ambivalent reactions to this film, just as I have, but for opposite reasons.<br /><br />What a muddle of historical meditations this awkward film stirs up in my mind!dical reconstructionist\\". However, such viewers soon run into a dilemma. Most of them are quite ready to side with Bush/Cheney over the so-called Patriot Act and the use of torture. Which agenda comes first? 9626343613|5.0|Even though it has been two hundred years since the world was first introduced to sisters Marianne and Elinor Dashwood's financial, social and romantic trials, their story remains for me, as fresh and vibrant as any contemporary story you might read of, experience yourself, or hear tell tale of today. I give full credit, of course, to Jane Austen. Her understanding of human nature and how to craft emotions and characters into an engaging story remains unparalleled. Add to that a delightful twelve hour and forty-three minute reading by the accomplished British actress Juliet Stevenson's polished interpretation of memorable personalities and you are primed for unsurpassed entertainment. Here is a brief description from the publisher:<br /><br />\\"When Mrs. Dashwood is forced by an avaricious daughter-in-law to leave the family home in Sussex, she takes her three daughters to live in a modest cottage in Devon. For Elinor, the eldest daughter, the move means a painful separation from the man she loves, but her sister Marianne finds in Devon the romance and excitement which she longs for. The contrasting fortunes and temperaments of the two girls as they struggle to cope in their different ways with the cruel events which fate has in store for them are portrayed by Jane Austen with her usual irony, humor and profound sensibility.\\"<br /><br />It is amazing to think that SENSE AND SENSIBILITY was Jane Austen's first published novel. As a debut author she showed incredible understanding of characterization and plot development. Many of the personalities contained in this novel remain the most memorable for me of her entire canon. The affability of Sir John Middleton, the persistent meddling of Mrs. Jennings, the droll indifference of Mr. Palmer and the malleable weakness of Mr. John Dashwood are played against the narrow greed of the unscrupulous Fanny Dashwood and her officious, spiteful mother Mrs. Ferrars. These secondary characters really make our heroes and villains shine, and withstanding the two heroines Elinor and Marianne, it is amusing to see how Austen plays with our emotions in guessing who the heroes will be and how the morality will play out. SENSE AND SENSIBILITY does have a few plot wholes and loose coincidences that readers will be raising eyebrows over, but it remains a novel wholly entrenched in the passionate joys of youthful love and emotional loss, cruel social snobbery and biting social reproof as relevant today as it was in 1811.<br /><br />Laurel Ann, Austenprosene, and withstanding the two heroines Elinor and Marianne, it is amusing to see how Austen plays with our emotions in guessing who the heroes will be and how the morality will play out. B0052GACNM|3.0|At the time of the release of the 2 best-selling albums of Sinatra's career, the Duets albums from the mid-'90s, I was elated. Many of us simply didn't want to believe, in fact rejected the unthinkable, i.e. that Sinatra was finished, so these latter-day gifts were sufficient cause for celebration.  And for the first month or two after each of the two releases, I played them repeatedly.  But soon the novelty wore off, and I had no interest in these concoctions, especially with the vast Columbia catalog, the priceless Capitol recordings with Nelson Riddle, the final extended chapter of his Reprise years all beckoning to be revisited.  Apparently, others felt much the same (recently, I've seen either disc going for a buck or two used).<br /><br />The Bennett Duets are less \\"tricked-up\\" than the Sinatra ones, which involved long-distance, call-in performances by his partners.  Moreover, Tony sounds almost as good as ever, much more vibrant physically and in control musically than Ole Blue was after 1990.  Moreover, the program comprises winning songs from the Great American Songbook, with Tony so confident and secure that, rather than being \\"carried\\" by any of his younger, more vigorous, partners, he's the one who makes up for their deficiencies with this material.<br /><br />Perhaps the track with the greatest interest will be \\"Body and Soul\\" with Amy Winehouse.  It's the most recorded popular tune in music history (check out the numbers at jazzstandards.com or allmusic.com), partly because much of its satisfaction comes from the challenge of executing it--difficult but ingenious and logical chords, unforgettable melody, mediocre lyrics--but Bennett and Winehouse (who sounds mature lightyears beyond her actual age) pull it off quite nicely.  Listening to Amy's first album--\\"Frank,\\" the one before her hugely popular \\"Back to Black\\"--it soon became clear that the very young girl at this time knew jazz phrasing and could swing. Once she finished the daunting \\"Moody's Mood for Love,\\" you knew right then that she was capable of delivering the goods in more than one style or genre.<br /><br />The one thing that may be slightly off-putting is Amy's attempt to sound rougher, more blas&eacute;, even a bit inebriated (stoned--or an affectation?), than either she or the song requires.  The same could be said about Lady Gaga's otherwise sparkling performance on \\"The Lady Is a Tramp\\" (what a travesty this number becomes when certain latter-day singers substitute \\"champ,\\" which not only wastes Lorenz Hart's clever, subtly ironic, lyrics but actually destroys the non-conformist, free and independent spirit of the lady portrayed in the song).  But Lady Gaga sings the song and the word \\"Tramp\\" with the most assured conviction (it's Tony who almost messes it up by (only once) substituting what sounds like \\"champ\\").  Though she could do without a couple of \\"growls,\\" Gaga proves that she's more than some sort of insubstantial hoax, one-trick pony, or year-round Halloween media creature.  Like Tiny Tim (whose musicianship won me over after the initial shock wore off), the person who plays the role of \\"Lady Gaga\\" is a wonderful \\"straight\\"performer, perhaps even a first-rate musician.  Sounds like she's listened to Sinatra.)<br /><br />The duets with Michael Buble, Natalie Cole and Dana Evans (Queen Latifah) are predictably right in Tony's wheelhouse.  He could easily make an entire album of arresting performances with any one of these musicians, who have jazz sensibilities similar to Tony's.<br /><br />The rest of the album will score big with some listeners, especially those who responded favorably to Michael Bolton singing with Pavarotti.  But to my ears the album tends to get a bit thick and mushy, even \\"injured\\" by musicians who simply can not swing.  I appreciated Merle Haggard's lengthy interview in the NY Times shortly after his \\"jazz\\" album.  Whatever one might think of the result, there was no question that Merle knew he was in unfamiliar territory, over his head and studying up as hard and fast as he could about the elocution, phrasing, and timing required to deliver a classic Gershwin ballad or swing tune and do it justice. Willie Nelson keeps revisiting the \\"American Songbook,\\" but I have yet to hear him adapt to its standards.)<br /><br />Bocelli and Groban contribute to the schmaltzy quality weighing down stretches of the album (The solo version of Tony's early signature song, \\"Stranger in Paradise,\\" is far superior to this new version.  Inexplicably, Aretha goes after a song that was one of the highlights of the first Duets album, \\"How Do You Keep the Music Playing.\\"  My advice: revisit the first version with Tony and George Michael or simply forget about it.  Aretha is one of the all-time great soul singers (starting out as a jazz singer, despite the popularity of her dad's recorded sermons), and she was delightful on the Sinatra Duets album, providing much needed life.  But here she takes on a freely structured song that goes no where unless the singer is able to take it somewhere.  She's in good voice but the effect on this listener is: \\"Been there, done that.\\"<br /><br />As for the others, if you prefer a duet for Harold Arlen's wrist-slasher, \\"One for My Baby,\\" make it any Sinatra version, or even the Kurt Elling-John Pizzarelli meeting.  On this occasion, the song, which is a musical soliloquy, or a Robert Browning dramatic monologue addressed to a silent auditor, and moreover a haunting cry of despair (one of Sinatra's greatest \\"suicide songs\\"), was a mistake from the very beginning (I can imagine the Arlen estate even being offended by this insult to the the memory of Harold Arlen and lyricist Johnny Mercer).  Performed as a duet by Tony and his youthful counterpart, it comes off as little more than slightly mischievous and lightweight albeit pleasant musical conversation.  In fact, there's a bit too much pleasant, pretty music on the album--nice-sounding voices, thick layers of sweet-sounding strings.<br /><br />But for Tony and the highlights on the album, the result, on balance, still amounts to a winner: 3 stars, a definite thumbs-up  though certainly not a high-five.  This time I'm not as quick to pull the trigger as was the case with the so-so (in retrospect) Sinatra Duets.  I could readily list (and produce from my collection) a dozen albums by Tony that trump both Duets dates--timeless performances, some with just Tony and the piano of Bill Evans, or Tony with Flanagan's or Sharon's trio, or Tony with Count Basie.  The man unquestionably knows the routine (to quote from the aforementioned Arlen song), and on a number of his solo recordings, he's gone beyond and above it--way above it, reaching artistic heights  on the level of the best by Armstrong, Astaire, Garland, Holiday, Crosby, and even Frank, singing his heart out just for the joy of it (and not a fiddler in sight).as he could about the elocution, phrasing, and timing required to deliver a classic Gershwin ballad or swing tune and do it justice. 0439023483|4.0|My youngest daughter (age 14) handed me the book after she finished reading it.  I read it in a weekend and found it to be a real page turner (5-stars).  I immediately went onto Amazon and bought the two follow on books.  This is definitely a book that parents should read before (or immediately after) their children have and then have discussions about the book.  I know that we all have above average intelligence children and they will be able to understand the book completely on their own -- but maybe discussing the book together will bring you closer.  You certainly will want to take the opportunity to help re-enforce what morality you believe your child should have.  This is the main reason that I knocked off one star -- parents really should read the book and discuss it with their children.  The book is dark.  If it were a movie, I would argue it needs more comic relief.  Judge your own child's personality and decide if reading dark material would bring them down too much or can they easily separate out a depressing story from reality.  Having read all three books (and yes, this part doesn't pertain to *just* The Hunger Games), it feels like there was one large book written and it was broken into two and a half books.  At the end of the two and half books there were lots of unanswered questions and the filler was added trying to answer those questions (stretching the whole to three books).  The first two books are absolute page turners.  The third book (which did not effect the star rating for this book) is a little choppy as it tries to cover questions that the reader might have.  I wasn't going to write a review until I read some of the other reviews.  Some have complained that there is no moral message to the book.  There is, but it is subtle.  If you miss the moral message it is possible that your world view is too different from the message.  And no, I won't say what I believe the message to be because I hate spoilers in reviews).  Some have complained thatthe characters are unrealistic in their goals and values.  If the characters were coming straight from our world, I would agree.  However, if you imagine the toll on priorities and how the dark world that Suzanne Collins created would wear and morph people's values over the preceding generations, I would say that Ms Collin's projection of people's values is certainly plausible.  I'm certainly not willing to run an experiment to find out.  Some have complained that the story is a blatant rip off of other stories.  These same people point to the beauty of J.K.Rowling.  While I thoroughly enjoy  Rowling's work, perhaps these reviewers should read Orson Scott Card's Ender's Game -- and realize what a blatant rip-off the Potter series is ...  No.  Even after pointing out the remarkable similarities, Card goes on to defend people taking the same plot line and weaving their own stories around it.  After all, aren't there only 7 plots?  In summary, if you normally don't read what your children are reading, I would make an exception in this case.  If you can, read the book(s) before they do.  If you can't, try to read the books anyway and discuss them with your kids.

Lack of vocal ability runs parallel to the non existent songwriting. The second flaw needing attention is the vocals. Boy does Anthony need massive help!   Some heartfelt recommendations for the band:   1-If you can't write standout songs get some good songs written by other people which you can embellish and make your own. The more obscure the better, people will think you wrote them.  2-Get another producer whose output you like. Trust their judgment and let them guide you. Check your egos at the door and do what they say as they can see a bigger picture.  3-Listen to some vintage old school funk and classic rock as inspiration and get down to business. Let those slow tempo fragments go away.   4-Make sure the vocals are the number one concern. Even if Anthony needs to be coached word by word with massive punch in's. Do that!    5-If need be (and this may be painful to fans), fire Anthony and get someone else who can really sing. He has always been the weakest member of the band.  I feel RHCP do have it in them to produce a stellar CD but factors need to change. Please don't disappoint again with a crappy CD like this! Get down to business and put some real effort into it and make heads turn. Work Harder! You can do it! You can do it! Please no more crap like this.

B002BWP4A6|4.0|&#34;The White Ribbon&#34; was shot in black and white; in a flat, deadpan style.  Upon reading several other Amazon reviews of this DVD, I observed that even if the reviewer didn't care for the film, they did admire Christian Berger's brilliant cinematography. The cumulative effect of tone and pacing is subtle, as if the director (Michael Haneke) did everything he could to make a suspense/horror film, without resorting to the use of any suspense/horror picture cliches.  This film is a testament to the corrosive power of family and societal secrets.  Its characters live in an oblivion of denial, in a village where certain key male authority figures wield power with an iron fist.  For the most part, the perpetrators of the various heinous events occurring in this picture are not revealed; these incidents are presented to the viewer &#34;after the fact&#34;.  On occasion, there is a silent, visual implication of involvement; for example, the scene where the boys watch the burning barn from their bedroom window; the group of children gathering outside the doors or windows of various homes.  Subliminal evidence of the &#34;revenge of the abused&#34; is directly and/or indirectly hinted at.  But ultimately, the villagers are working very hard at appearing to be innocent (the white ribbon, after all, stands for innocence).<br /><br />In Chapter Two of this DVD, a child attempts to lift the veil covering the face of a dead woman.  He is then comforted by a man sitting nearby in the dark; watching this scene, I suddenly feared that the father was sexually abusing the child.  Although the plot is easy enough to follow, in that particular scene, I wasn't 100% sure who the dead woman was, who the child was, and who the man sitting in the dark was (the director purposely kept him &#34;faceless&#34;).  And so I assumed that those characters were the doctor, his former wife (now dead), and the boy was his son.  The point being--whether or not one knows who these characters are, the effect of this scene is chilling; it is the most disturbing one in the entire picture.  Following that scene, the older sister of a little boy explains to him that everyone must die; he struggles to understand mortality.  That is probably the most touching scene in the film.<br /><br />There are two characters in &#34;The White Ribbon&#34; who are equally abhorrent in their roles as abusers; the Doctor and the Pastor.  The Pastor is bigger monster of the two, as he hides behind his mask of moral superiority.  The Doctor, although reprehensible, is at least aware that he is a monster.  When the Schoolteacher (who, along with his fiance Eva, are in the minority of villagers that could be described as &#34;good&#34;) confronts the Pastor near the end of the film, the Pastor warns the Schoolteacher not to reveal his theory about who has been committing the crimes in the village.  It's as if the Pastor metaphorically becomes a black-winged demon in that moment.  And of course, the Schoolteacher realizes by this point that there is no place for noble intentions in a village that is suffocating in a stew of its own vices.  So often in life, the worst among us are perceived to be the best; what is evil is mistaken for good, and vice versa.  Although I think this is a good film, and one that gave me the chills as I reflected back on its scenes--I still wish that Lars Von Trier's &#34;Antichrist&#34; had won the 2009 Palme d'Or at Cannes, instead of &#34;The White Ribbon&#34;.<br /><br />Stephen C. 0425241505|5.0|Why I Read this Book: Book Blogger buzz is the one and the only reason I read Dragon Bound. The buzz surrounding this book made it practically impossible for me to ignore. I didn't read any reviews; I just saw the buzz circulating on Twitter. The main consensus was that while Dragon Bound is PNR but it isn't your typical PNR. And I'd have to agree with that statement.  What I Liked: Dragon Bound is not a book I would have normally read had I come across it at a book store or the library. One reason being I haven't read a PNR with dragons in it. I'm one of those people who generally sticks with what they know and love and for me, that's vampires and werewolves. (I really need to expand my reading tastes.) Another reason being I was a bit intimated by the complex world building just from reading the blurb. I know that I, as a reader, don't do well with complex world building. It's not what I look for in a book; I don't want to have to think too much. I've got so much going on in my brain already, I don't have the capacity or the focus for all the extra details that come with a complex book. So I tend to stick with PNR with mild world building so that reading remains an (easy)enjoyment for me.  The first thing that I loved about the story was the coin that was stolen from Dragos. The `take a penny, leave a penny' concept was genius! I knew from that point on that I was going to love this book.  I really liked Pia's character. She is Dragos' calm and serenity. He's an entirely different being when he's around her. She tries to teach him to ask instead of demand. She brings out a kinder side of him though he's still the same tough and fearful alpha ruler. She doesn't put up with his temperament; she demands respect and he gives it to her. She challenges him in ways he's never been dared challenged before and he likes it. He's jealous, possessive and obsessed with her - all of which is entirely new to him.  Another thing I liked about Pia is that she didn't take Dragos' hand outs. She wasn't there to take advantage of his financial or power status. She can take care of herself and wasn't dependent on anyone else.  I love the mystery behind Pia's half wyr-half human heritage. The revealing was stunningly beautiful. I was a bit confused as to whether or not Pia herself knew what her wyr-half was. She seemed to know that she needed to hide as much information about herself as possible but I don't think she knew the exact why to it.  I loved the dream Pia has towards the end and the meaning behind it. I can't wait to see how that story arc plays out.  Here's one of my favorite quotes that shows Dragos' vulnerability:  *****  \\"I meant to lay a trap for you with that dream. I trapped myself instead.\\"  *****  What I Didn't Like: There wasn't anything I didn't like about this book.  Overall Impression: Dragon Bound is the type of book I wanted to slowly ease into. It deserved my full and undivided attention and that's what I gave it. I wanted to grasp every single detail I could to take it all in. It was like eating fine piece of chocolate from Godiva - you don't just gobble it down and start on the next piece. No. You savor the flavor and enjoy the textures and indulge your senses to achieve the most exciting and exhilarating experience possible. (OMG, I can't believe I just compared reading this book to eating chocolate! But you get the gist.)  I got Dragon Bound from the library but I am definitely going to get a copy for my shelf. This is definitely re-read material here and coming from me, that says a lot. And I'm definitely looking forward to reading Storm's Heart.

1609611543|5.0|This was an interesting book - it starts with a history of wheat. I hadn't realized just how genetically modified the wheat we have today is when compared to wheat of 100 years ago (or further back, for that matter). In order to increase yield and resistance to pesticides, scientists have been playing around with wheat for some time, without really taking the time to understand what long term effects this genetic modification might have. Davis then goes on to look at how wheat interacts with the body - like the fact that wheat consumption has the same brain effect as opiates - as well as looking at Celiac disease, diabetes, the body's pH, and aging as they are impacted by wheat. From there, he goes on to talk about what the wheat free life looks like and why he feels it's worthwhile (and really, \\"quit eating wheat\\" is drummed into your head at least once on every page - not without back up information, but still, the point is very clear - wheat is bad, as are wheat substitutes). There are mentions of actual clinical studies as well as anecdotal evidence from Davis' practice - but in the end, he makes a convincing case to at least give living wheat free (and again, not just gluten free, but really just giving up wheat) a good try.

0143113100|5.0|This is a very good book, though perhaps not written as a page turner.  This information is very valuable for two reasons.     First, it could potentially change the way we look at diseases of the mind, and what are the options for treating them. Like so many other good works of medicine, this book clearly shows that not all treatments need to be drugs and surgery. The brain has a built-in flexibility never before imagined. But up to now we have not guessed this, and have not known what kind of stimulus is required to activate the brain's own inherent abilities to change and heal itself. He points towards what some of the new therapies might be.    Second, one can ponder, how and why the brain has the kind of flexibility it does. In one example, people blind from birth learn to see through their tongues. A video camera is strapped to the subject's head. The camera image is converted into electrical signals, those signals sent to a small disc, and the disc placed on the subject's tongue. In a matter of days the blind person's visual cortex is interpreting the signals from the tongue, and the people can \\"see\\". This all touches on a much larger topic, that our brains have far more complexity and ability than is necessary for our survival in the life we lead. If our brains only evolved to give us better abilities to find mates and increase territory, then why would we have staggeringly complex neural abilities that do not relate to finding mates and increasing territory?    --Michael Clarage  [...]

Sam has always been special to Grace, and she spends as much time has she can during the winter outside watching for him.  When Sam shows up as a human bleeding on her doorstep, Grace steps in without hesitation.<br /><br />Shiver is a book that doesn't really have any particular 'enemy' to it, except the ever-approaching cold that could force Sam to turn back into a wolf. Despite this, I could never stop reading as Sam and Grace's tale is one that kept drawing me back. I had to know if they'd get their happily ever after or not. B004K8WPUQ|5.0|I've been a Canon user and Canon fan for many years, but Canon video cameras always seem to have some sort of sound problem. In the case of Canon DSLRs with video capability, the problem is the loud and annoying hiss that occurs when you use an external microphone in a quiet setting. The first time I used my new Canon 60D to make a video, I quickly realized that the onboard microphone, while adequate for perfect circumstances, was not a good fit for outdoor use or pro-style interviews.  I decided to buy an inexpensive $45 shotgun mic - big mistake - which produced the loudest ambient hiss I've ever heard on a recording. After a bit of research, I found that the 60D and other Canon DSLR cameras (and no doubt other brands as well) almost always produce a notable degree of \\"quiet room hiss\\" when used with most external microphones--and of course it's usually worse with a cheap mic. Further research led to further frustration, with all the \\"experts\\" suggesting that quality in-camera recording is just not feasible on DSLRs and you should buy an expensive independent recording system and then \\"sync\\" the sound to the video in post. What?!?!? I don't work for Sony Pictures, I just want to do some good quality commercial work for Internet posting. Then I ran across a YouTube video of a guy testing Rode microphones. He said that, after posting his video of a test with a Canon DSLR, Rode contacted him and told him that by setting the Rode Videomic Pro's dB switch to +20 and adjusting the manual sound level setting on the camera to about 20%, he could achieve a virtually hiss-free recording. Thus I ordered this mic from Amazon. It arrived on-time and in perfect shape, I ran a series of tests under various conditions, and I couldn't be more happy with my purchase. Interestingly, the suggested DSLR setting is right there in the Rode manual--perhaps they added it after the YouTube video was made.  Bottom line, if you're shooting video with a DSLR and you want simple, high-quality sound for a reasonable price, this is your mic.  Buy anything less, and in my opinion you will be unhappy with the results.<br /><br />One small caveat: This is a professional mic with a 10 year warranty, but it's not for people who don't take care of their equipment.  The mini-jack cable is thin and could easily catch on something and break or be ripped from the mic if you aren't careful. The \\"rubber band\\" shock mounting system--while clever and effective--is easily pulled from its mountings and could be easily lost. (One extra set of replacement bands comes with the unit.) In other words, be prepared to handle with care, and you'll no doubt get years of use from this excellent pro microphone.sound for a reasonable price, this is your mic.
